RATING: 7.5
Rated based on game potential, overall design and expandability.

Numerous rules mistakes and game mechanics that start to be discussed in the forums make the rating provisional until it can be decided if the game has major flaws or not.

Rating lowered : I don´t think the game has major flaws, but still too many little nuisances keep coming that will have to be answered in future FAQs. Annoying.

Played 6 times in both roles and 3 out of 5 stories several times. The game is great but has a lot of room for improvement that will keep me waiting for more. Replayability might start to be a problem a little bit too soon.

Rating lowered : Replayability might not be such an issue. Starting to see the shines of a great game design, and learnt to live with the concessions made. Sadly, the careless customer support received is the main motive for this rating drop. I don´t feel optimistic anymore about the future fixes to the abundant mistakes made with the first edition of the game

But given the theme and the type of game this aimed to be (and I hope it will be someday in the future...) is still in my top 10. Will probably change my rating when the first expansion comes out.

Edit August 2013 : Rating raised since last Expansion addressed quite a few issues and put the game in the right track. In any case, one of the most played games in my collection, so tha must mean something...

RATING: 6
El concepto de Ameritrash hecho juego con la ambientación de la obra de H.P.Lovecraft. Mansión a explorar y buscar pistas, sucesos, bichos raros que salen y te hacen perder cordura o vida, y diferentes personajes estereotipados a elegir con buenas variables de poderes y objetos, etc. El juego es asimétrico llevando un jugador al "guardian" de la mansión haciendo la vida imposible al resto de personajes/jugadores que cooperan para llevar a cabo su misión. En mi opinión es un buen juego de este tipo, pero a mi no me llaman nada, y se me hace tremendamente larga la partida y repetitivas las mecánicas y acciones muy típicas de estos juegos. Azar y dado por doquier, y ninguna sorpresa de mecánicas. La inmersión en el juego podría mejorar bastante, y no es ni de lejos la de un  juego de rol, que en estas circunstancias le da mil patadas, salvando las evidentes diferencias.

RATING: 9
[2-5] What a richly thematic game! I was hoping for a classic, adventure/story-telling game that really plays upon a haunted house vibe and I wasn't disappointed in the slightest. I always wanted to get into the Call of Cthulhu RPG but haven't ever taken the plunge. I'm so happy that it went over well and am looking forward to plays in the future. Here's hoping for continued release of stories and demons!

It's replaced Arkham Horror for us because it's so much more thematic and just easier to get to the table. Setup time can be a downer but the system is fantastic, especially with painted miniatures.

Expansions owned: [thing=108150]Forbidden Alchemy[/thing],  [thing=131751]Call of the Wild[/thing], [thing=101921]Season of the Witch[/thing], [thing=110699]Til Death Do Us Part[/thing], [thing=124640]House of Fears[/thing], and [thing=131339]The Yellow Sign[/thing].

RATING: 8
I LOVE THIS ONE!  The theme *OOZES* from the game!  Production is fantastic!  PLASTIC!!!  It feels very Lovecraftian and works very well!

Despite my enthusiasm, only rates an 8 for a few reasons:
(a)  It's on the longer side (a tad long for what it does).
(b)  Requires one person to act as the GM (they know the whole story in advance and are running the game for the other players and not really against them - it's up to the GM to ensure a fun play experience rather than "win").
(c)  Has a long set up (requires preparation in advance and then 15-30 min. to set up the board when you do play).

RATING: 5
A melding of Betrayal at House on the Hill with familiar mechanics from Descent was not at all a bad idea.  Sadly, it doesn't really come off for me.  Mansions brings a little more narrative coherence to Betrayal's wandering around and picking up stuff, but overall I found that narrative functional, prosaic and lacking in Lovecraftian flavour.

In Mansions, the Keeper seems to have the most fun and the lion's share of the decisions.  The player characters really are just moving around finding clues, items and puzzles.  If you're a puzzler you'll probably find those in Mansions too straightforward.  If you're not, you probably won't welcome their distraction. As with Descent, I found myself thinking that with this sort of time investment required, I'd much rather be doing some actual role playing.

As usual, Fantasy Flight has done a very nice production job, with atmospheric artwork and the miniatures of the large monsters particularly good.

RATING: 6.8
I wanted to like this game so much, but it turned out to be so much more hype than substance - at least after my first play.  

It's dripping with a great theme and has some interesting mechanisms but, well, you know how some games take a long time but don't feel like they do?  This wasn't one of those games.  

Our five player game had four newbies playing against one person with one game's experience under his belt.  It took over four hours - even allowing for a short break for food.  The problem wasn't just the length though.  It was the huge amount of downtime in the game.  Some of that might come from the inexperience of the game with our group, but it's clear the game has a problem in that regard.  

In addition, the combat mechanism is pretty lame to me.  Some people have touted it in their reviews as being a strength of the game.  I guess those people and I have very different tastes.  At first I thought it just pointless because the "random" element of the card draw determining how you attack made planning seem utterly pointless.  Then, as the game dragged on, and on, and on, I realized that while it seemed random it's actually rather predictable and even though it means you CAN actually plan, it's somewhat dull.  There were very few interesting decisions when playing as an investigator.  Perhaps being the keeper has more interesting decisions?

I'll give it one more try, but if it doesn't seriously redeem itself then it won't get a third try.  There are too many other games I like or want to try to waste time on an overly-long and vastly overrated (at the current time) game like this.

RATING: 5
Rating after 1 play:  As a fan of thematic horror games like Last Night on Earth, I'd been watching this for a while and was very interested in the high ratings and comments.

At first glance the game components, the board and the minis were amazing.  The artwork and production beautiful and you could feel the Cthulhu theme everywhere as the game commenced.  But that was where things started to unravel for me.  

It may play well when you are the master, because you have all the information available to you and plenty of decisions to make.  But as a player, your options feel limited and in many respects, insignificant.  There just aren't enough meaningful decisions to make and the characters feel inconsequential as they walk their way through semi-scripted scenarios.  The way the game "scripts" you to run back and forth across the board to achieve your goals was frankly, downright annoying.  Whilst the puzzles proved novel mechanism at the start, the rapidly became a pointless distraction to the flow of the game.

The poor layout of the rule book and the sheer volume of cards and variations included for each of the multiple situational conditions added considerably to the game length, which was way too long.  Playing out at 4+ hours, there just wasn't anywhere near enough to maintain my interest.

I can see how some people would love this game, particularly for the pseudo-RPG nature of it, however for me it just fell flat.  I probably should give it another try, but in reality, there are too many other games out there I'd much rather play.

RATING: 3
I have played Call of Cthulhu for over 25 years; this game was a must play so I was looking forward to our game. However, it was a major disappointment for the prime reason of how luck driven the game is. It is almost totally dependent on where the cards are; over half of the randomly placed cards on the board were "you find nothing of interest" and we turned these over with the tedium of trying to find something useful. The dice rolls killed the interest after a while. Failing adds to the game and can be entertaining, but having at least 12 successive failures from the start just is not fun. As a group, when it mattered we did not make a single key roll. Going the wrong way (a 50:50 choice) delayed us about 6 turns and in the end we jacked it in after wasting 60 minutes to find the first clue. 
A game like this should have some degree of player control, and this was lacking. It is far too linear, and replayability appears to be minimal once you know the general story. A big letdown, without the expansions (which may have sorted some of the problems out) as it stands, this is a classic bad game.

RATING: 7
Aunque he de reconocer que es entretenido le saco un par de pegas:

Se tarda media hora aproximadamente en montar el tablero de juego. Los investigadores ayudan durante 5-10 minutos, luego deben dejar al guardián a solas para que monte el resto de componentes "secretos". Durante 20 minutos el guardián se queda solo murmurando para sí mismo mientras los demás se divierten (o aburren) en otra parte. Una carta colocada en el lugar equivocado puede arruinar 2 horas de partida. Es recomendable que el guardián lo revise todo antes de dar por finalizado el montaje (eso son otros 5 minutitos...)

A por la segunda pega...

A las historias las falta más texto de ambientación que permita a los jugadores enterarse mejor de que es lo que está pasando, por que está pasando y como acabará todo si no logran detener los planes del guardián. Las cartas de pista son muy pequeñas y el poco texto que cabe en ellas es insuficiente para darle ese toque de investigación que este juego pretende vendernos. Los textos deberían venir ampliados en el libro del guardián y las cartas solo ser un breve resumen o un simple recordatorio del párrafo que hay que leer. Los eventos en lugar de complementar la historia a veces la hacen más confusa o directamente no tienen nada que ver.

Ejemplo: "Aparece un zombi, envuelto en llamas, corriendo por el pasillo" -WTF!!

RATING: 4
Rating based on just one play.

I'm not sure if the genre is not for me, or if I've recently just played a few poorly designed representatives.  It's also possible we played a particularly bad scenario, but I doubt it.

Lots of bits and cards and items, but very little strategy.  Rarely did I feel like I had to think about my actions or make any choices.  I'd wait for my turn and then walk towards the nearest event.  Then something good would happen and I'd get another card (half of which I never ended up using), or something bad would happen and I'd roll some dice to see how bad.

Then the keeper would take over and do something annoying like move me backwards a few spaces -- so I'd have to cover the same ground next turn.

I want to like heavily themed games like this, but I need to feel like I'm facing some real choices.

RATING: 4
Dungeon-crawl game. Has some interesting concepts like the puzzles you've got to complete to open rooms, but when it comes down to it it's just another co-op game. It's better than Arkham Horror, but I still won't be begging to play it, and will likely try to avoid playing it if possible.

I think my issue with games like this is that the strategic decisions are minimized, and luck is maximized. Whether or not a plan works isn't so much contengient on whether or not it's a good plan, but whether or not the scenario is designed well and the dice favor you.

Better is like Warhammer - where if you have a good plan, a few poor dice rolls don't really hinder you (a lot do, but still your victory status is primarily decided by how clever you are during the game).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Story, story and more story, this game is full of it, and that is a good thing. 
One player controls the keeper and the rest of the players control the Investigators.

Monsters and cultists can be summoned by the keeper, rooms can burn, the lights can go out, other rooms might be locked and the investigators have to solve puzzles to enter. 
The keeper also plays during the investigators turns using mythos cards and trauma cards and thus making the task more difficult for the investigators.

Investigators gather weapons and artifacts to fight monsters, but most importantly they find clues. Once the investigators find enough clues (depending on the story) they discover the whole plot, and understand what the keeper's objective is and what they must do to win the game.

The setup is a bit longer than most games, placing the mansion tiles and the keeper placing the cards are the things that take the most time. Everything else is faster.

RATING: 2
It is a blender. The atmosphere is big in the beginning but the more you fight the more it becomes obvious that this game has not much to offer gameplay wise. The investigators are played by this game - they did not have many options on their turn. Some scenarios are broken or just uninteresting. The combat system which sounded good at first is really annoying as well: On the one hand you draw through the whole deck very fast so you read the same cards over and over again and on the other side you do not have any possibility of influence. In Arkham Horror you can adjust your player values, in MoM you can only add your luck value to your specific tested ability. Meh...

So all in all: Play Arkham Horror instead for a much better Lovecraft experience!

RATING: 6.5
Another game in the FFG Lovecraft Mythos series. The idea behind the game is nice and it delivers on gameplay. The game is fun to play and tense till the end. But again it suffers from the FFG disease of less than thematic game elements that really spoil the game for me to a certain extend. In my first game I lost my guns in the very first turn stating I forgot to bring ammo. Really?! That's just awful design as it basically nullifies the choices I made in setup. Making players feel like their choices don't matter is never a good idea. The puzzles and treasure hunt feel are well done though. Components are also very nice. So this game gets a thumbs up from me in the end. But only just.

RATING: 9
In my endless and often fruitless search for thematic games I finally stumbled into Mansions of Madness, a superb game that impressed me both for its thematic depth and gameplay: even though I’m not a Lovecraft fan (and neither a horror genre’s) I revel in playing Mansions of Madness for it’s such a successful crossover between an RPG and a boardgame that it manages to create an immersive and dripping atmosphere just by taking the best out of both genres.
Sadly though, this blend’s most notable casualty is a character’s interpretation, because the game - however long - is too short to allow it: in order to survive instead everyone needs to do everything, for the better or worse.

I already had some experience with Betrayal at the House on the Hill, a game that looks similar to this one and never fails to appease me: yet it fades when compared to Mansions of Madness, for this latter is more and better in everthing. It must be noted though that the two games appeal two different audiences or moods: Betrayal at the House on the Hill is more of a lighter, shorter and Goosebumps-like cooperative game, while Mansions of Madness is a deeper, longer and full-immersive horror game, made harder by a dedicated keeper/GM player.

All in all, Mansions of Madness is the perfect game for any special occasion, because it requires some time, involves all the players alike and gratifies all of them equally. Nonetheless, its complexity is its only drawback as well: actually it can’t be played repeatedly, because each scenario’s replayabilty is pretty limited, at least with the same players.

RATING: 5
Fully co-operative, RPG-lite (experience) type of game, but it's largely a race to explore the map and solve a mystery before time runs out.

UPDATE 2014: I'm no longer a fan of this game.  I like some of the puzzles, and I usually like one-vs-many games; however, the luck of the cards, die rolls, red herrings and painful searching for objectives (not knowing if you're making progress) makes the game really slow-paced and frustrating for me.  Largely, it's a timed race to explore the map and reveal clue/objective cards.  Maybe it's a better game if you go in with the mindset/approach that the monsters are too difficult/scary to kill so you're going to run past/away from almost all of them in an effort to reveal clues as fast as possible.  Note to self: Fitch and Sarah would like to try with all investigators splitting up and running room-to-room rapidly.  Our 2014 game was very long and we failed to reveal several clue cards, resulting in an unsatisfying game.  Also, the replayability of M.o.M. is very poor.  Playing the same scenario with slightly different clues/objectives just sounds lame.  Ultimately, the game is designed to sell you expansions if you don't have the creativity/time to make your own scenarios; it might as well be called "Expansions of Madness" because it'll become a money pit like CCGs, then just collect dust because you've played all the scenarios.

RATING: 7
Needs the right mood. Sometimes it doesn't work at all. Should be played with a dedicated GM and not competitively. If it works, it's really good and offers a feeling no other game I have does with its great atmosphere,intense finish and engaging story.

The game starts quite slow and builds up tension. Investigators usually rush out to find out what's the goal of their game and the Overlord hinders them. After the win condition is revealed things change drastically. Investigators are near madness / death and try their best to finish the game, while the overlord schemes the final blow.

There's a lot of badness that can happen.
 1) It's easy for the investigators to screw up early (without knowing for a long time). The most typical would be: They take too long (usually needlessly fighting monsters); They misinterpret the overlord's behaviour and don't protect important characters/assets; They ignore explicit text they were told (Fridge!).
 2) The game may end without the investigators even knowing the goal of the game.
 3) Randomness can drastically screw the chances of the investigators/overlord resulting in a too easy / impossible game.

Overall a good game.

4pl 5pl

RATING: 7
Juego ambientado en el universo lovecraft. Los jugadores (salvo uno que hace de master o "keeper") son investigadores que llegan a la mansion y allí deben ir descubriendo pistas para determinar cuál es el objetivo que deben cumplir para ganar el juego. Cuando llegas a la casa no sabes qué hay que hacer para ganar. Es un juego en el que lo más cuidado ha sido la ambientación. Cada partida es una historia. El juego incluye unos cuantos escenarios y presumiblemente, se podrán incluir más. El jugador que hace de keeper es el que lleva el hilo del juego, maneja los eventos, etc. Es colaborativo y si ganan los investigadores, todos comparten la victoria por igual.
No es mi tipo de juego, pero reconozco haberlo pasado muy bien jugandolo. Es importante, como siempre, la implicación del resto de los jugadores para tener una experiencia plena.

RATING: 7
I'll start by saying that this sort of game really isn't the sort of game that my group and I like.  Merchants & Marauders, Ravenloft, Arkham Horror have all fell flat.

However, I can't help but like this game.  The combat simplicity is pretty nice, and the skill checks are done in a very simple manner with a single simple die.   So it actually feels pretty streamlined for a game of this type.  The theme is pretty accessible too (spooky house, ooooooOOoo...)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I was almost afraid to play this.  I love Arkham Horror, but can't get into Elder Sign.  It looked as though they'd split AH into two games, the mechanics going to ES and the RP aspects going to MoM.  In fact, that's not far wrong.

This is NOT a roleplaying game, but it does synthesize the essence of RPG experience into board game form rather nicely.  It manages to hold on to theme (in a way that ES does not), but it does sacrifice the subleties of a real RPG.  (In fact, experienced roleplayers often have to told repeatedly "This is not a subtle game.")  If you get a clue, you need to follow it then and there, not wait for the right time.  If something is going on somewhere on the map you can't see, or the party is split up, you don't assume "fog of war" and pretend you don't know what's happening elsewhere.  

I'm pleased, if not totally convinced, by the quasi-replayability of the scenarios, but haven't had cause to try it out so far.  I suspect I'll wind up buying the expansions.  Still, I like the [i]feel[/i] of this game.  It's bite-your-nails tense, and full of those oh-man-we-are-so-dead moments as the Keeper picks up a handful of threat tokens and proceeds to surround the investigators with eldritch Bad Stuff.

Best part of all, for me: in 2-3 hours, I can have all the fun of running an RPG (well, most of it) without the hours of prep I would normally spend.  And that is awesome.

RATING: 6.5
Ashar loooooves this one, and I must agree that in terms of theme, it is king.  Even Arkham Horror doesn't come as close as MoM to making every game a narrative with memorable moments and the feel of impending doom.  I'm less hot on it, probably because I am always the keeper.  Ah yes, narrative is fairly easy to come by when one person plays the DM...  So I have never had the satisfaction of uncovering the plot and facing the dread of the Old Ones.  The keeper is fun to play, no doubt, but also has a good amount of bookkeeping to do.  I'd rate this higher if the stories themselves were better.  Some have bitched about limited replayability, but that is not a big deal for me (unless you play it once a week, you'll still have months and months of play even with only 5 stories).  No, the real issue is that the stories are somewhat inconsistent.  A few feature one psycho, or one witch... while others feature everything from cultists all the way to ol' Soggy itself.  Playing a scenario with one or two baddies is just not that much fun, while scenarios with a ton of baddies are too top heavy.  More balancing would benefit this game.  We got some paint so we could paint these minis, but so far it has not happened yet.  Since Ashar refuses to let this game out of her iron grip, I'm sure it will happen eventually.

RATING: 3
It does boggle the mind the prodigious quantity of crack that must have been smoked to have come to the conclusion that there was functional and interesting game in this box. There are nice miniatures. There is flavorful flavor text. There is nice art. All of that is good. There are some boring, repetitive, and athematic puzzles, plus a flavorful but insanely tedious combat resolution system. But there is no game. If I want flavor text I can read a book. If I play a game I want to make some meaningful decisions, and have a story of some kind evolve from those decisions. It doesn't have to be a lot - Castle Ravenloft works for me, especially at a touch over 1 hour - but there is nothing at all in evidence here, plus it runs 3-4 hours. Mansion of Madness is just a dungeon crawl, and a torturously bad one at that. Back when Battlestar Galactica came out, I had some hope for Corey Konieczka. He hadn't produced any professional-level games yet, but he seemed to be learning. But Mansions of Madness is complete amateur hour. It flagrantly violates too many basic rules and structures of game design. If the nice flavor text and art is enough for you - and for many people it is - more power to you. For me though, I do require an actual game.
---
Another truly horrendous rulebook from FFG. Whoever their rules editor is over there is in desperate need of being fired. Really, it boggles the mind. Rules-writing is not easy, but it's not rocket science either and it's nowhere near as difficult as they make it seem. I give GMT flak from time to time for their lousy rules - and believe me, they deserve it - but FFG is even worse. I failed my horror check about a third of the way through and let myself be taught.

RATING: 3
[floatleft][imageid=935943][/floatleft] [b]Mansions of Madness[/b] - [i]Mansions of Madness[/i] should feel intimately familiar for connoisseurs of the horror-RPG-inspired board game genre.  The game largely takes its cues from [i]Betrayal at House on the Hill[/i], but, for better and for worse, beefs up that model with additional complexity and then stretches the experience over hours rather than minutes (too be fair, I've been told that my two sessions of [i]Betrayal[/i] were atypically short).  Although the game contains some notable improvements over its predecessor, it also brings quite a few significant drawbacks to the table.  In most instances, the game's failures are a product of novel, well-intentioned ideas generating poor incentive structures for the players at the table.  Not surprisingly, then, I find the game both promising and nearly unplayable (by me; I'm inclined to think that this game would appeal to those who are already predisposed to like this sort of experience game).

Like [i]Betrayal[/i], [i]Mansions of Madness[/i] uses a one-against-many dynamic in which one player controls the monsters against which the other players are struggling.  In [i]Mansions[/i], however, only the evil player knows his objective from the very start of the game.  This information may not be revealed to the investigators for hours, during which time they wander around semi-aimlessly looking for clues.  

One of the big innovations of the game, of course, is the use of mission-specific clues (along with items and bad guys) to produce a coherent narrative throughout the adventure, rather than simply a string of random, open-ended encounters ("Oh look, I found a mysterious book...again.").  However, the clues are extremely heavy-handed, meaning that no real deduction or investigation is required.  For example, our first clue was "You smell something burning in the Kitchen"; the clue in the kitchen was "A trail of sooty footprints leads from the Kitchen to the Hallway."  The forced narrative often deprives you of real choices and makes you feel much less like an investigator and more like a pawn in some poorly constructed slasher movie.

The game exacerbates this sense of fatalistic determinism by locking essential clues behind doors that require special keys (usually only available at the site of the previous clue).  Wandering off alone is not only a bad idea in the general sense, but it is also usually a fruitless endeavor.  The game simply won't allow you to shortcut the story, which again means that the players are simply along for the ride.  

The key system actually has another substantial drawback.  By treating each key as an item, the game only functions if players work together to get the single player carrying the key into the right room to unlock the door to the next clue.  Yes, I realize that [i]Mansions[/i] is openly a cooperative game.  But the investigator's turn plays out in an unsettlingly Pandemic-like fashion with all players so concerned about hurting the team with an obviously bad choice that no one acts unless and until a consensus on the entire turn is reached.  So the overall feeling from the investigator's side becomes one of not really playing your own turn (due to the need for concerted action), followed by several minutes of downtime while the evil player spends a multi-step turn staring at cards that the investigators aren't allowed to see, spending a currency that the investigators don't have, moving monsters in asymmetrical and unpredictable ways while aiming for goals of which the investigators are not aware.

I want to be clear: I really did appreciate some of the game's innovations, but the game also felt like it was constantly stepping on its own toes with mechanics that slowed the pace and made the wrong trade-off between narrative and gameplay.  A great contrast here is the game's use of combat cards and skill points:

When you choose to attack a monster (or when one attacks you), the evil player flips through a deck of combat cards until he find one applicable to the situation (e.g., Sharp Melee Weapon vs. Monster).  The card then tells you how the encounter plays out, including any needed die rolls. The idea is that this allows each fight to have its own narrative and to proceed in slightly unpredictable ways.  It's not just the same-old "declare, roll, deal damage." But the emphasis on storytelling comes at the cost of meaningful decision-making and game flow. First, the flipping is time-consuming; by the third or fourth deck reshuffle, you start to wonder whether it is a worthwhile mechanic. Second, the card you draw determines any number of things, including which attribute you roll against and the damage you might deal or take.  A character with high Marksmanship wielding a gun and attacking a monster that it has been fighting in a closet for the last two turns might flip a card only to discover that the story has now decreed that she was trying to shoot while running and her attack will be tested against her low Dexterity instead.  Failing the roll, the gun blows up and the player takes damage. Because of such possibilities, the card deck essentially makes the decision whether to attack or evade a monster nearly incalculable, and again forces the narrative upon players rather than letting them generate it.

At the other end of the spectrum, the game gives each player "skill" chits that they can expend to improve die rolls. On the one hand, this seems like a great idea.  Players can accumulate these chits throughout the game and get "lucky" just when they need it the most. Very thematic. But the scarcity of the chits meant that the players at my table insisted on knowing all the possible outcomes of each roll before they decided whether this was a critical moment worthy of a chit.  Not only did reading out the options mean added downtime for the reading and0 added downtime for the player to conduct his/her risk-reward calculation, it also (and much more importantly) produced a break in the narrative that took me out of the game entirely. I hate, hate, hate hearing in advance that  you have a 60% chance that spooky noise you heard was nothing and you calmly walk away but a 40% chance that a zombie jumps out of the mirror, you panic and take two damage and a Horror point.   Here, the seemingly clever design seems to beg for informed decision making that results in the destruction of the narrative. (If I ever play games like this again, I will insist that roll-dependant outcomes are not read unless and until triggered by the roll, even though it makes things like Skill Points much less useful.)

The accumulation of so many minor-but-annoying design choices over such a lengthy game (ours ran 3+ hours and we quit with two turns still to go) became a bit much. One of the reasons I rated [i]Betrayal[/i] so highly is that it is a fun, 30-minute romp.  Over such a short timeframe, flaws are easy to ignore.  At about 1/7th the pace of my [i]Betrayal[/i] games,  "romp" is not an applicable word for [i]Mansions[/i].  By the end of the game, the things I found questionable so sufficiently outnumbered the things that I found redeeming about the design that I find myself unlikely to play again.  

It's a tantalizingly promising game, and one that I think could be interesting to the right players with the right experience and the right inter-personal dynamics. But it doesn't have enough going for it to survive a failure on any one of those criteria, let alone all of them.

RATING: 9
I love love LOVE this game!  Arkham Horror with a much more story-driven interface that focuses on a more RPG-like aspect.  It's not high on replay value, despite what you may hear.  It's also a BEAST to setup (30-45 minutes, on average!).  The role of the Keeper is also a finnicky one, and difficult to master.  Despite any of that, I'm in absolute love with this game!

Side Note:  I had 3 of my Investigator figures falling of their bases upon acquiring my copy.  I emailed Fantasy Flight in hopes of receiving replacements for those pieces, and was pleasantly surprised when I received a full set of replacement investigator figures along with a handwritten thank you note.  This possibly secured my loyalty to Fantasy Flight for a LONG time to come!  :D
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Based on 1 play with 5 players:

VERY hard to rate this after just one play. Seems like things could vary dramatically based on the scenario. Rating is very, very tentative.

I REALLY like that Fantasy Flight is taking steps to make their games shorter and more streamlined. A definite step up from Arkham Horror in that regard.

The one thing that rubbed me the wrong way was how objectives get revealed. In Betrayal, I'm fine with having the first part of the game just be about exploration because you're constantly having interesting encounters or gaining equipment, etc., and it doesn't take that long for the haunt to begin. Here, an hour or two can go by before the investigators even figure out what their objective is... if they ever do at all. After awhile, it just feels like aimless wandering.

The theme is also a little bit of a letdown. It's fine, but for whatever reason I just didn't get the same level of immersion I found in both Arkham Horror and Betrayal.

On the plus side, taking a cue from Descent, the Keeper is a great addition. Having one player actively working against you makes things feel less random and more heated. And, compared to Arkham Horror, it takes away a lot of fiddliness from things like monster movement etc.

Oh - attacking is pretty neat. The deck of cards definitely makes things feel more themeatic compared to other games. Just worry that things might feel stale after you've seen most of the cards.

All in all, the jury is still out on this one.

RATING: 6
I painted the miniatures, and its FF so of course its easy on the eyes.  Setup time is prohibitive, and play time is sometimes prohibitive, sometimes surprisingly fast.  Scenarios are entertaining and have a nice variety of theme.

Experience with this game has been a real mix.  Investigators often feel that there is insufficient guidance, and that the challenge is insurmountable.  In several instances, this has been true.  Also possible is the investigator trudge, where the investigators journey is tedious and time consuming without being particularly fun or challenging.  The all star scenario can be a great deal of fun, with the investigators barely averting catastrophe at the eleventh hour.

I've only played the first three scenarios, but those multiple times.  It appears to me that the first is the most balanced, most likely to be the third type of experience listed above.  The second is either insurmountable or a trudge, and the third is merely barbaric in the horrors it perpetrates upon the protagonists.  This gives the appearance that only the first scenario was play-tested.

The components are great, as I would expect from FFG, however the chits are unending, and the rulebook lacks coherent organization.  This is all standard fare for FFG.

Overall I enjoy the game greatly, but feel guilty about dragging the group into a potentially 3-5 hour game which ends up being a great disappointment by the end.  It's too much of a gamble to try it out hoping to have a successful and gratifying experience.

RATING: N/A
 Mansions of Madness provided for countless memories that became some of the best lines, moments, and memories of 2011. Based on the rich Cthulhu Mythos, each play through tells a horrific story that could be written as a short story to account for every move, decision, and challenge that faces the investigators. Even after numerous play throughs, the Mansion continues to introduce new secrets, items, challenges, puzzles, traumas, insanities, enemies and more. The game also successfully creates an amazing and horrific atmosphere - even when the investigators find weapons and powers, there is constantly a sense that they could lose, go insane and turn against their friends, or be killed at any time. Just as players begin to abandon hope, they can often win in the final moments of the game, creating an epic climax that leaves the everyone thrilled - and occasionally ready to start over and play again. The game creates a cooperative environment for players, but also gives them a thinking, evil, unpredictable, human foe to overcome.

RATING: 8.25
If you took a typical D&D set-up, mixed in all the story and atmosphere of Arkham Horror, and then simplified the whole experience, you would have Mansions of Madness.

The set-up and rules explanation for this game can be very lengthy, so I'd recommended you play with an experienced keeper that's already set up the game.  Because the actual gameplay moves along quickly, and if the players get really involved there isn't even much downtime.

The potential issue is replayability, but Corey Konieczka has cleverly added multiple story possibilities within each main story.  I do, however, think once you're keeper for a story, it would be hard not to feel spoiled for future iterations of that story.

Expansion potential is big here, and I expect we'll be seeing more stories in the near future.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It's really a great game if your looking for some Lovacrafting RPG-lite gaming. The scenarios, which make it, are generally on point and really give you a feel of the Lovecraftian atmosphere. There are a few that are a little lackluster, you can find the reviews online to weed out the bad scenarios.

My group played regularly, we went through about 8-10 sessions of the game and we still want to go back for the expansion that I picked up. One suggestion is start off with he first one to get your bearings of the game, the game will last a couple of hours. Also there is a lot of set up involved. I would generally prepare the night before and get everything organised so that I could reduce the set up time when game night came along. Reason why that set up is so long is due to the fact that each room has item cards associated to it. These cards are placed specifically there to guide the player to the clues and help them solve the mystery so sorting through all the cards and separating them for each room takes some time. This job is left to the keeper to keep the scenario secret form the investigators, taking some time. 

With that all said we had a blast with the game and if you have the time and a good group it is definitely worth the investment.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I guess I just don't really appreciate the Mythos theme as much as others.  If it were a good game, I could overlook that.  It's merely an okay game.  Maybe an introductory game as a stepping stone to Descent: Journeys in the Dark.  It has the same feel, but plays in a shorter time.  

Another thing I disliked was the linear path of exploration.  The heroes must obtain certain "keys" in order to unlock another part of the mansion.  So, it's not as if the heroes can truly go exploring on their own.  

Furthermore, the die-rolling is frustrating when you don't roll well.  My character learned a spell, and despite using a Skill Token prior to casting, I never (in 3 attempts) rolled a successful attack with it (only needed a 7 or below).  

Finally, I appreciate their attempt to make the creatures' information easy to spot on their bases, but the figures took up way too much space on the board.  It would have been better to have smaller figures and a cheat sheet for the players with the monsters' attributes.  

My love of Ameritrash seems to be more geared for the epic-level games and less so for the smaller-scale, tactical, mini-games such as this one.

RATING: 8
Very much a story driven game with some tactical miniature gameplay. I do not feel the bread crumb/clue based scenario progression is too linear and I feel the setup adds enough randomness to keep it interesting.

Investigators will be on the losing end more often than not since the Keeper has more access to information and scenarios can end abruptly for how long the game plays and the long setup. That being said, the atmosphere of the game pulls me through any imbalances so far.

Setup is really lengthy though.

RATING: 6
A story driven RPG lite romp in the Cthulhu universe. Plays shorter than Arkham and it does provide a more consistent narrative than Arkham Horror, however it still plays a little clunkily and mechanistically - typical of FFG. The plot's of the scenario's I played were pretty simplistic and just not quite detailed enough to really come to life - casting one player in the role of the DM is a bit wasted as the player runs the monsters in combat rather than the plot.

Concerned about the replayability - they need to bring out more scenarios and hopefully these won't be too expensive - print and play (with a download charge) would be acceptable for me.

In the end just wasn't as much fun as Arkham Horror and that linked to the replayability issue - SOLD

Did think about keeping this just for the floor plans of creepy houses and cellars and the like which are excellently realised and could easily be used in other games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I think that years of tinkering have finally paid off for FFG. Starting with clumsy Talisman-style games like Arkham Horror, they have tried out lots of variations: Doom, Descent, Android, and Middle-Earth Quest. As these evolve FFG has been keeping the things that work and tossing out the nonsense. 

Mansions of Madness is the best yet of its type. It's a clean and zippy iteration on the old Sorcerer's Cave/Black Morn Manor series. It does a great job in narrative. The sub-systems keep it from becoming boring, but are not distracting. The first one I can really recommend!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is a great way to get that Arkham fix in less time with much less rules burden.  I love that really only the Keeper needs to have a firm grasp of the rules.  The other players only have to deal with choosing from a few options, so the game can get underway very quickly, assuming that the setup has already been done.  The scenario-based gameplay is a nice complement to the random experience of the full Arkham Horror game (or Betrayal at House on the Hill) and I will be lucky if I play it enough to get bored with the included scenarios.

UPDATE Jul 2016
This is a nice design, and I think superior to Betrayal at House on the Hill thanks to its more prescripted, but still variable narrative.  The problem I have with it is one of personal taste.  Because it is so much about the story, as the keeper I just can't bring myself to play my hardest.  I WANT to investigators to succeed, so I can't really enjoy playing the bad guy.  I think maybe the new co-op version might be more to my liking.

Comment Update:
Played friend's copy.

Very heavily thematic, which I love, flavour text flooded the game pretty much everywhere. The game has text, but not too much like in Last Night on Earth. Another thing I like more when compared with LNoE is that there is less bookkeeping in MoM. I seriously adore this.

In LNoE, seems like there are too many cards or event that modify a certain value which require players to always refer to and remember. I just hate it sometimes. The same problem occurs in Castle Ravenloft and Conquest of Planet Earth.

In MoM, battle happens super quick and test on your abilities is done with the roll of a die, super quick as well. Only gripe, the puzzle can be a deal breaker sometimes. If I'm in the mood, it can be fun, but once I have too many puzzles to solve, it can be daunting.

●Friend owns it

RATING: 6
Man, this one was incredibly tough to rate.  We don't actually own it, so each time we've played, our friend has been the keeper and selected the scenario.  

Let me start with what I like about the game. Thematically, the game looks awesome and is pretty fun to play.  My husband is an excellent role-player (while I am quite terrible), so the game gets silly pretty quick as I try to keep up with him.  For the type of game it is, it plays in roughly the right amount of time (3 hours or so).  

Now, I orignally thought this game to be an 8 or so and was considering getting it for our collection.  But here's my issue:  I've played 3 scenarios and had a pretty bloody awful time on two of them.  Opinions are divided as to what went wrong.  My husband feels some of the scenarios are just designed poorly.  We played one last night that basically punished us for doing well.  My team had moved through three quarters of the board before the objective was revealed (get to the starting location before the last event) and because of this, we did not have enough time to backtrack.  I was fine with losing, and I didn't think the scenario was that 'terrible'.  

I think our bigger issue is shotty play by the keeper.  I know mistakes will be made, but we've had issues with wrong equipment being randomized, improper monster decks attacking us, horror checks all but forgotten, you name it.  When you go through the trouble to play a 3 hour long 1 vs all game, it drives me absolutely batty when the one doesn't take the time to do it right.  I really think this game would be better if someone else in my group gave it a go as keeper.

Non nego che qulache gruppo possa trovarlo un ottimo gioco ma nel mio ha floppato alla grande.
Strizza l' occhio al gdr ma non ne raggiunge le vette di libertà interpretativa e decisionale e di contro gli aspetti da gdt (strategia, tattica, competizione) sono enormemente "diluiti".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an imperfect game that I enjoy if played differently than originally intended. That is, I enjoy the game with a solo/coop mode variant made by a fan (kittenhoarder) or by playing more as a DM and less as someone trying win a game. 

The reason being, I firmly believe that a Lovecraftian game should be cooperative and not competitive because once you introduce a competitive situation, the theme of inevitable madness, chaos, and futility underlying the genre goes out the window because people want to win for the sake of winning and the enjoyment of the hopelessness of the theme goes out the window. It shouldn’t get anywhere near that but rather everyone enjoying when things going horribly wrong. 

I’m an avowed fan of Lovecraft (see my article, [blogpost=27416][/blogpost]), so I’m inclined towards the material. What I really like here is how they made the scenarios true mysteries. As an investigator, you don’t even know what the victory condition is until well into the game. You’re just walking into a creepy house and searching around and having scary stuff happen to you. You might very well die. Can’t argue it’s not thematic!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
there are tons of things in the box...the Lovecraft theme is far stronger here than in Arkham Horror and the session time is more managable.

Set-up time from a cold start is quite long and very specific, but that is to the benefit of future replay of the 5 scenarios.

Both the Investigators and the Keeper need to stay "in character" reading the flavor text to tie all of the things going on here in context of the story.

two annoying things...the repeated Horror checks for monsters can get tedious, and the combat deck "draw till you match your attack type" is clunky at best.

Overall not bad, Still prefer Witch of Salem when I crave the end of everything, but Masions of Madness does deliver on its premise.

NOTE: If you want to get the most mileage out of this one; DON"T READ THE CARDS WHEN YOU ARE NOT PLAYING...they are "things man was not meant to know."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
B&B/purchased 2010/arrived 2011

The box says 2-5 players, and strangely enough we played the first game with 6 because we misread the box.  The next game was played with 5, and all those involved said there was no difference between playing with 5 or 6.

9 different people played the two games tonight, and this is what they had to say:   
[b]Everyone liked the game (myself least of all)[/b].  Everyone said they would be up for more games, and I have been asked to bring it again next gaming night.  I will play it if someone pulls it out, but I will not suggest it myself.  

[b]Excluding my rating, everyone put it in the 6-8 (out of 10) range.[/b]  Those who have played Arkham Horror, agree AH is a better game, more complicated and with more options than Mansions of Madness.   MoM is a good gateway game to Arkham Horror.

One friend who played the Keeper in both games tonight, has played many games of  both Descent and Arkham Horror, said that Mansions of Madness is a mix of the two.  He rated Mansion of Madness an 8 out of 10.  

Positive comments by the players:  Lots of  re-playability, like the forced ending that keeps the game within reasonable time limits, favorably impressed that draws are possible, interesting looking, love the miniatures, and enjoy the story line.

Negative comments:  Too finicky,  puzzles not hard enough,  didn't get to do enough in the game, needs an organizer for the components, daunting rulebook if never played this type of game before.  I seemed to be the only one who objected to the downtime between turns.  The time I got to play compared to the length of this 2-3 hour game, was a very small ratio.  I didn't care about the story line, which held the interest for others.

I took an entire afternoon to just punch, bag, and label the numerous different kinds of tokens, tiles, cards, and miniatures.  I glued the figures to the bases and after play switched the baggies for organizing containers for the many components. 

I am keeping the game, but I will not be buying the expansions which are sure to follow--which is unusual for me.  I am just not that big a fan of this genre of game.

RATING: 7.5
Most thematic game I've played

Pros
-I can easily sell this game to any player and get them interested/hooked on the theme alone (and as a plus, it's extremely easy to teach, each player basically gets 2 move+1 action and has to walk around and search for clues)
-Immersive with the right group of people (lots of flavor text helps), and a real sense of mystery (even how players win the game are a mystery to all except the keeper)
-Very cool use of physical puzzle pieces to resolve certain events (e.g. unlocking a door requires you to rotate or swap adjacent tiles until it matches)
-Generally high quality components
-Numerous scenarios (still retains high replayability due to variations within a story). Players can also create their own scenarios with the map tiles/cards

Cons (pre-expansion) 
-Somewhat limited use of clues (in guiding players to the objective)
-Questionable balance of some character abilities and design on some cards
-Game is not faithful to source material (monsters have general abilities, do not relate to their literary counterparts in terms of abilities or special powers)
-Some annoying monster miniature which easily fall off their bases (bases are swappable) unless you glue them down
-Variable scenario quality
-Chance of finding nothing is fairly high when exploring (remedied in expansion)
-Chance to break the game in some rare situations (e.g. you break and discard the key you need to proceed in the game or the person you need to kill is moved out of the game through some other means and is no longer available)
-Limited replayability in the long term with provided scenarios
-Some gamers may not enjoy the theme and the lack of a clear objective
-Long game length
-Physical puzzle can results in long downtime for players not involved

RATING: 2
Our group tried and tried to play this. Somewhere in here is a decent game (a 6 or 7), but unless this is the only game you play and you can memorize every little thing you, are going to get something wrong and ruin the experience. Whether it be in the specific scenario you are trudging through or general game rules something is going to break and unlike a paper and pen RPG the system is too rigid for satisfying quick fixes that seem fair.

Case in point, one time the game, after spending an half an hour setting the adventure up, ended in two turns because of the way it was set up. Investigators entered a room that was only a couple spaces from where they started and it ENDED the game. This followed several looks to see if something was set up wrong and behold, that was just one of the ways that it could be set up and the Keeper's choices put it there. Ended in 15 minutes. We didn't touch the game for a long time after that.

The straw that broke the camel's back was when there seemed to be no way to get to areas because a item specific to the adventure had no clear way to be used. The Keeper scoured to rulebook for the answer only to find well after we gave up that it was in the set up in a tiny paragraph that was overlooked. So yes, it was totally our fault for missing it, but why wasn't there more indicated on the card. I mean really, why did everything have to be that clunky.

Really the time and effort you have to put into this thing and the satisfaction you get out of it, even if it "works" and you see it to the end is negligible; you just never really end up having fun.

We play a wide variety of "heavy" games, most of which I'd rather be playing.

RATING: 3.5
I should sooooo love this game.  I only played the school scenario, but here's the rundown...

Components: EXCELLENT!  Especially with the painted minis the game owner subbed in.

Theme: Not my favorite.  Not really a fan of horror, less so of Cthulu.  However, I am a big fan of games that tell a story, and it does do that.  It does a better job than most games that try to tell a story.

Game play:  Ugh.

In theory, I should love this.  RPG-like game with unique characters running around picking up stuff to work together to solve a problem.  Sounds like something that's exactly what I'm looking for.

In practice, it was like spending 3 hours watching somebody try to drag a key down a hallway because the keeper kept pushing him back over and over and then he got too stupid to find a door.  Multiple times.  In 3 hours, the extent of my combat experience was throwing holy water at a monster one time.  I learned to cast spells at monsters but never had a chance to use it.  I had tons of boosts to my skill checks that I never had the opportunity to use.

To top it off, at the end neither side reached the goal and the game was unspectacularly declared a tie.  (Or 'both lose' - whatever.  Same thing.)

It did have the feel of a narrative... but in a shaggy dog story type way.  Left me torn between "I should so love this game" and "This is so painful." 

I've rated it a 3.5 based partially on the components.  Gameplay alone was a 2.

Everyone else said this scenario was the worst they'd played and it was a lot better than this, but it was so bad that it's going to be tough to get me to try it again.  Given the number of other great games that are out there...

RATING: 10
I feel I need to clear something up about the replayability of this game.

Story 1:
 
3 questions = 3 diffrent objectives, 4 diffrent ways the house can be seeded, 12 possible combos (not including the random seeding).
 
Story 2:
 
4 questions = 3 diffrent objectives, 8 diffrent ways the house can be seeded, 24 possible combos (not including the random seeding).
 
Story 3:
 
5 questions = 3 diffrent objectives, 16 diffrent ways the house can be seeded, 48 possible combos (not including the random seeding).
 
Story 4:
 
4 questions = 3 diffrent objectives, 8 diffrent ways the house can be seeded, 24 possible combos (not including the random seeding).
 
Story 5:
 
6 questions = 3 diffrent objectives, 32 diffrent ways the house can be seeded, 96 possible comobos(not including the random seeding).
 
 
 
This is if you play with the same group and only one person is the keeper each time.  If you are properly mixing up questions/stories each time you play, I seriously doubt you will ever reach the point where you "know the mansion by heart".  
 
Every clue has 2 possible locations it can be determined by if you anwers questions 2-3,4,5,6 A or B.  Combined that with the 'chart' above (First question determines objective (A, B or C), questions after that determine clue locations.
 
I don't really see a group memorizing that any time soon.  Granted, if you keep picking the same story + questions (keep a list of what you have done so far) they will grow used to the flavor text and story and start to learn quickly where each thing is, but if you mix it up properly this should not happen unless your playing this back to back extremely often.
 
This also does not include easly made other stories, either offical or unoffical. You would not even need a expansion pack to make many more stories with what you have.

RATING: 3

---------------------------------------------------------

I've now played each of the scenarios and my awe of the game has lessened and my annoyance has grown. I really like the theme and the idea of a keeper as opposed to random events as in Arkham Horror but once again it is astoundingly clear that Fantasy Flight has not done much, if any, play-testing. In practically every scenario the keeper can win the game (100% of the time!) if he spams a single reusable ability or if he draws the right mythos card (of which he'll be drawing one or two every single round). For instance, in several scenarios the players must get through a manhole cover to continue the adventure. The only way to open the manhole cover is by finding a crowbar. If the keeper plays a mythos card that destroys the crowbar, the game is over; players lose. That is just one example of many, many unbalanced and obviously untested elements to this game. Once again, I love the theme. I love the scenarios and basic mechanics of the game. But the lack of balance is nothing short of unforgivable.

RATING: 1
This game represents about 4 hours of my life that I will never get back. The only horror I experienced was being stuck in a game that wouldn't end.
Initially, I would have described this game as an adult version of Hero Quest, but it turns out that in contrast to this game, Hero Quest was fun to play.
This game suffers from so many things it's hard to know where to start. It wants to be an athmospheric RPG, but the players cannot contribute to the story-telling. It wants to give a sense of horror through sanity points, but those just end up being a second kind of hit point without real meaning. The madness the GM can unleash on the weakened minds seem insulting to the player rather than scary. The puzzles the players have to solve are ridiculously easy for the players and frustratingly cumbersome for the characters to deal with. The game heaps on mechanic after mechanic as if to give depth to the game, but instead just buries it. I can see no redeeming points about this game at all.

RATING: 8
The game is about racing to incrementally reveal a horror/mystery story (i.e. win conditions) while trying to cope with more or less subtle horrific events. This idea is simply marvelously implemented. It's like a hard and fast CoC scenario that focusses on the basics. Nice components, great game art, fast to get into. It is a story based game, i.e. it is quite linear; as far as choices are concerned, they are situative (ad hoc), not really tactical, and certainly not strategic. 

As the game has received ample of pre-publication hype, let me focus on the negative points. The problem I have is that the keeper's actions are somewhat generic, and it is at times far from obvious when and how to pursue one's available actions in order to win. As it turns out, some game plots require the keeper to immediately and consistently pursue a specific task. But as he is not allowed to look up on the event cards that pace the game, it is almost impossible to understand in advance what to do. Of course, it becomes somewhat easier with experience to grasp the demands of the task at hand, but it is still far from obvious. Moreover, the investigators pick their characters independently of the game plot. Thus, it can turn out that a non-fighter group needs to tackle a shoggoth, or, alternatively, a guns blazing investigator kill team finds itself in a race-for-time plot and is utterly slowed down by puzzles. Balancing the investigators is not the solution, because often it is useful to break up the group, and the dumb fighter character can end up having to pick locks, and the  smart loremaster ends up having to defeat the monster. Of course, this will also be a flashy story, just one that was doomed to fail from the get go. 

Still, even though MoM is not exactly an amazingly balanced game, it certainly is a VERY entertaining story based game. If everyone at the table is happy to play in order to see how a faceted horror story unfolds, almost every play will be a memorable experience - even if the ratio between decisions made and relevant impact on the game is not as high as one might wish.

RATING: 6
Had fun but I want to play again before finalizing the score. Great components and theme, but I don't think I like it as well as arkham, and it has a few other issues that I think will bother folks, the puzzles feel a little out of place and if you don't pay attention to the clue the game goes stupid on you.  That being said it's very exciting that such a narrative based game is out there and I look forward to playing it again.

EDIT:  Second play, this time as the keeper/DM, and the game's flaws weren't just because we were unfamiliar with the rules first time around.  The players seem to have very little control on the outcome of the game.  As the keeper, your choices aren't particularly interesting, and if the players luck into discovering the quest related elements quickly, you probably can't win.  The problem with this in a story driven game is that the luck in mansions does not create tension or a story.  This is the exact opposite of arkham, where every time the dice are rolled or a key card drawn you are on the edge of your seat.  No one is going to tell stories about the time they lucked into going to the correct room really early, as opposed to an arkham situation where a player got lucky and killed a space vampire with her bare hands.  Really too bad, because a lot of the parts here are great, the combat system is solid, the components are gorgeous, but the quest narratives drive the game instead of the players driving the story, and that, combined with rules that are more complex than arkham, leave me questioning if I would play this instead of arkham horror.  I don't think I ever would.

RATING: 5
I wanted to love the game.. but it didn't happen.

Not knowing the objective builds tension on one hand, but leaves the investigators with nothing else to do, then follow the trail of clues and grab everything in reach. Not very strategic (i guess the game aims more for immersion)

The combat is quick but players are disappointed that their weapon did 5 damage with one card flip & 1 with the next.. and if they loose the scenario because of that.. bummer.. would have prefered a die-roll.

The game is really hard on the investigators, since they don't know what the objective is, the gm can simply wait & build up his threat. Then at the exact opportune moment he can release all hell on them & most often win the game. With other games i don't mind when the group looses but with this one i felt bad smashing them down. Maybe because it's more about theme & horror.. so if they loose because of one hitpoint, everyone sits around and thinks.. well..

Another thing is often you realize "well i can't win the game anymore" or "they can't win the game anymore" because they/the gm would need to move more spaces than he actually can.. so should you stop right there or play 3 more rounds although you already know who won..? Unsatisfying.

Other than that great production value & theme.. i just had huge expectations..

RATING: 4
This one is difficult to rate.

On the one hand, I really enjoy playing this game.

On the other hand, MoM is very clearly missing large sections of the game -- the bits, rules, and extra scenarios put off until the inevitable expansion is released. This is even more blatant than in [thing=17226][/thing].

By way of contrast, [thing=10547][/thing] in its second edition contains everything one needs to play for dozens and dozens of times.

While this may be good marketing on the part of FFG, it makes me feel as if I bought an expensive demo version of a video game. I'll have to pay more down-the-road to upgrade to the full version.

The errata is not too severe, but troubling nonetheless. The most serious problem I foresee is a lack of replayabilty once the five scenarios have been played.

Later: The lack of replayability when playing with one group of gamers is definitely a problem. Players begin to choose which character they'll play and which options they'll take based solely on the scenario to be played. I suppose characters could be assigned randomly, but that seems kind of bogus. Dropped rating from a "9" to a "7" accordingly. Perhaps a big book of scenarios might convince me to raise my rating.

Even Later: Many of the scenarios (especially 4 and 5) are not well playtested or borderline broken, leaving only about 10 plays. I've kind of given up on this game. I'll use it as a play aid in my Call of Cthulhu RPG sessions, but as a stand alone board game -- pass. Pity, because I laid out some serious cash for this one. [i]Tant pis.[/i] Dropped from a "7" to a "4".

Traded for [thing=40354][/thing].

RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
As [thing=66356]Wrath of Ashardalon[/thing] is to [family=190][/family], so [i]Mansions of Madness[/i] is to [family=310]Call of Cthulhu[/family].

Both board games maintain the general theme while stripping out the free-form role-playing elements by reducing player actions to a small set of discrete choices.  The end result is a feel similar to the mechanical surface of the source role-playing games, allowing the players to tell shallow stories without the usual RPG prep-time overhead.  (Veteran RPGers will scoff at this, and they should, since experienced players can whip up a far deeper and more satisfying game in moments with nothing more than [i]paper-pencils-dice[/i] and the Magic of [COLOR=#CC0000]Im[/COLOR][COLOR=#FF6633]ag[/COLOR][COLOR=#FFCC33]in[/COLOR][COLOR=#00CC00]at[/COLOR][COLOR=#0033FF]ion[/COLOR].)

That said, [i]Mansions of Madness[/i] *is* the [i]D&D[/i] board game version of [i]Call of Cthulhu[/i], with all the wonder and nerd-rage that such an eldritch mash-up implies.  Monsters are there for shooting at, and not as a last resort...  It's best to ignore the two-fisted comic book treatment of the source material and just enjoy it for what it is.

It's a board game opportunity to engage in a plot with clues, puzzles, and—if you're lucky—a broken leg and suicide.

The combat cards are nifty; when a player says, "I'm shooting at the thing at the end of the hallway," it's rarely a simple Marksmanship test—the player can miss and hurt themselves in the process or find the thing suddenly right next to them.  And even when you "win" you can wind up dead in the process.

[b]A note to future Keepers:[/b]  [i]The story portion of the game really shines when players narrate their actions with a bit of detail—encourage this.  Also, it really helps if you embellish and customize the flavor text on the cards.  When fighting a cultist, for example, replace all references to the foe with "cultist" and have him or her do and say "culty" things.  The game more than meets you halfway on this stuff, but if you can supply the other half it's a full-blown hoot.[/i]

The game is lavishly produced, with miniatures that actually beg to be painted.  (Disregard the "chicken leg" Shoggoths and Mi-Gos masquerading as the Hamburgler.)  Also, it's not nearly as complicated as the rule book would have you believe.  It's refreshingly simple compared to [thing=15987]Arkham Horror[/thing].

After one play I'm giving it an 8—though I expect the rating to rise with more plays.  We're all inveterate hams and lifelong RPGers, so this drubs all the right nubbins for us.

UPDATE:  Sure enough, this game is great.  The only thing holding the rating back from shooting to an auto-10 is the fragile nature of the interlocking systems.  They're all neat, but a single error in one place can cascade through and break the game, usually to the detriment of the Investigators.  (Small mistakes magnify as the game progresses.  In our first two games the win came down to time management, and early errors in movement rates meant the Investigators couldn't win.  Familiarity with the systems should mitigate this.)  This forces the players to slew hard left into RPG territory and hand-wave the issue away in order to save the game, something that feels unnatural and suspect in a board game.

Also, the puzzle mechanic is genius fun.  It's not that the puzzles are challenging—they're pretty simple—but I'm a huge fan of props from the game world made actual size.  The result is a nice bit of immersion.

UP-UPDATE:  Revising upward to a 9.  Our last two games have been real nailbiters, literally hanging on a final die roll.  Both times the narrative elements narrowed to a satisfying climax for all players—when this game works, it works really, really well.  Fantastic system.

[b][size=11]Keeper-6, Investigators-2
[COLOR=#CC0000]No Win-1[/COLOR][/size][/b]

[b]The Fall of House Lynch[/b] — 2 plays

5p, Investigator.  Keeper win.
5p, Investigator.  Keeper win.

[b]The Inner Sanctum[/b] — 2 plays

5p, Keeper.  [COLOR=#CC0000]No win.[/COLOR]
5p, Keeper.  Keeper win.

[b]Blood Ties[/b] — 1 play

3p, Keeper.  Investigators win.

[b]Classroom Curses[/b] — 2 plays

4p, Keeper.  Keeper win.
4p, Keeper.  Investigators win.

[b]The Green-Eyed Boy[/b] — 1 play

5p, Keeper.  Keeper win.

[b][thing=110699]'Til Death Do Us Part[/thing][/b] — 1 play

4p, Keeper.  Keeper win.

RATING: 10
Of all the games in my collection, Mansions is the game that gets requested to be brought back to the table the most. And that remains true for every group I've played it with, regardless of how different those groups tend to be. In spite of the longer setup time, in spite of the weight, in spite of the game's length, everyone I play with loves it. 

Mansions just brings with it such a rich, gratifying game experience and impresses upon each player a lasting memory of the narrative that developed over the course of the game. Years later friends reminisce about that disastrous time we opened the freezer, or about that time the whole house got set on fire, or how they solved the runic puzzle at the last possible moment before getting overtaken by a horde of zombies. The game works wonderfully for making the stories it tells come alive for those playing it. 

For facilitating a night of great social interaction and memories and fun, Mansions is without a doubt the best game I can think of. Easily one of my favorite games.

RATING: 7
Sounds interesting, A mix of Betrayal at House on the Hill, Descent, Arkham and CLUE on steroids!? 

Could be cool indeed even if somewhat rehashed, lets see how streamlined it comes out...

Edit - Well Jeremy's review says mostly streamlined and it looked that way to me, EXCEPT the puzzles as mentioned.  I still wonder about a little downtime which was not mentioned (will depend on player# and will be HUGE during puzzle mode I suspect) and am [i]especially[/i] concerned with the replay value as it stands (once again we need a FF expansion fix, a nasty pattern that is though this one was planned this way....). Also it seems like in many of these games (FOD comes to mind), the bad guy has the more interesting decisions to make and has been also mentioned the annoying repetitive checks and lack of protection if an Investagator all seems a little off, no way to mitigate some of the DM nastiness (again fixpack in expansion).  

Why don't they do more outside playtesting to flush this shit out during the design process instead of waiting for us to always expose it??? How many times does the same pattern need to repeat, its been [i]years[/i]?  I know they can do better.  

Cautiously Optimistic, it should be cool in a BAHOTH kind of way and I do like puzzles in general so the game being one big one seems fun. Definitely want to play, maybe own. I expect it to be a better Betrayal than Betrayal and like it there is a mystery to solve but a slightly more interesting one, probably a 7.5 - 8 for me

Edit - Played, its mostly like Betrayal as suspected and I like it a LITTLE better than Betrayal, which is less than I expected. Decisions are not that interesting as an Investigator and this to me was the biggest weakness, overall the game did run well otherwise. This is probably as good as a game like this is going to get, "it is what it is" applies.  

The theme is strong of course and the game being one big puzzle is cool (though your just searching all the cards trying to get lead to the one that reveals the objective), however I do figure it will be good for maybe 3 plays for me which wasn't enough to buy it (not due to replay, but do to waning interest).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The components are very high quality and add nicely to the theme.  One game played.  I was the keeper and there were four investigators.  The investigators ran into serious trouble as they didn't have any fighters in the group.  I did warn them about this but they didn't coordinate character choices.  That said, we were all expecting more of a Betrayal on the Hill exploration rather than a constant slugfest.  We ended up cutting it short as it was clear the investigators were going to lose.  It ended up being a bit of a downer.  I think it could be fun but the investigators need to be warned to be ready for lots of fighting.  One mistake I made was to keep the keeper action cards secret so they didn't know what I could throw at them, although I don't think it made much of a difference since I gave them advance warning about the most important one.  Something that also differed from Arkham Horror is that the characters mostly get weaker (damaged and traumatized) as the play goes forward rather than stronger.  If you like building up a character, this doesn't seem to be the game for you.  Also took longer than expected.  Anyway, looking forward to trying again and hopefully having a better time with it.

Second game: Went better this time.  Party of four with two fighters and two thinkers.  They left a trail of dead monsters.  They did still get hung up on the monsters.  I had to give them some coaching and pulled my punches on the cards but they still got frustrated.  I think it would go well if the keeper only got half the threat tokens.  Then the keeper could go all out without overwhelming the players.  That said, they also had some bad luck. We also didn't have a good sense of just how time strapped the players are.  I think they really need to just charge forward and mostly ignore the monsters, contrary to the player guide posted on this site that suggests standing and fighting.  The keeper can basically just generate a new monster every single turn when there are four players.  I really want to like this game but it's a little off somehow.  For example, even when you've got a gun, there is a tendency for the fight cards to ask for a roll on something other than marksmanship, resulting in a lot of misses or dropping the weapon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
2012-10-29

Initial Rating: 8.0 (February 2012)

As I love Arkham Horror, I thought I would like Mansions Of Madness and I did. The miniatures are gorgeous and very well painted (in the copy we played). Also it is nice that you can use them in an Arkham Horror game and the components are top notch (as expected from FFG). The game is comparable to Betrayal at the House on the Hill but the theme (and miniatures) gives an edge to Mansions of Madness.

The gameplay is deceptively simple so you can explain the game quick enough but it can take 2 hours+ to finish it.  You need the right group of people to properly enjoy this (as Arkham Horror) but it can be a lot of fun. To really enjoy the game, the keeper (and players in a minor capacity) need to role-play this, Reading and tweaking the text to adapt it to the situation at hand. What I really like about this is how the story develops. 

I´m not sure about the puzzles, I need to play more to see how I feel about them. For now they feel novel enough.

My main worry was that the game has only a few plays in it, but that was before I was the keeper. Now I know that each scenario is variable and have 3 different objectives and that is very neat. I want to play them all. Another aspect of the game I don’t like is the lengthy setup, comparable in time to Earth Reborn. I’m not sure which is larger but in my second game I took more time building the map and choosing the cards than playing the actual game :/.

Another potential problem in this regard, is that the keeper has to set up the game correctly, if he messes up and the mistake is not noticed right away, the game can break wasting all players´ time. 

For now I’m downgrading this to a 6.5 rating as this is not a game I want to play every week, but I´m usually willing to play it. Also, it is a good game with the right group. I want to (and will) play it again.

Current Rating: 6.5
