=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mansions of Madness is sort of a Cthulhu-themed version of Betrayal at the House on the Hill, without one of the players turning on the others (although there's no reason that couldn't happen...). Players explore the house, going from clue to clue until the objective is revealed, at which point, things get very intense. One player is the keeper and runs the house and the monsters and the various nefarious things that get thrown in the investigators' way.

It's a fun game. One thing that is unique in this versus similar games and is worth mentioning is the way in which locks are handled. To pick a lock, the player needs to complete a puzzle. There are three types of puzzles to solve, which all start with a randomly placed set of tiles. You solve the puzzle by rotating, swapping and trading out tiles to achieve a certain pattern. The trick is that you can only make as many moves as your character's Intellect. Even if you have adequate intellect, the player's skill in puzzle solving is still a big factor. I think this is a brilliant little piece that uses BOTH a player's skill and a character's traits together. I was dubious about in-game puzzle solving until I saw it at work. While the game as a whole is not terribly unique, that single mechanic within the game is absolutely inspired!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We don't play the stock scenarios at all anymore, but BGG's fully revised scenarios for the base set have always resulted in outstanding sessions for my group.  The POD expansions have been great, too, with only minor tweaks.  I don't anticipate any time in the near future when it starts gathering dust on the shelf.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
My experience with this game was disastrous! It's hard for me to know just what I think due to a keeper who did a horrible job of explaining the rules, and mislead me into thinking it was a 90 minute game when in fact it took 4 hours.

On the flip side I really want to like the game. It has a very interesting theme and all the components are beautiful and engaging. Upon reading the rules (after the fact) and reflecting on the game I don't think the actual mechanics and game play are very good. I don't mind longer thematic games when they're done right, however this game seems to have some serious issues for me.

Not letting the investigators know what's in the keeper manual makes no sense to me. I have the same issue with Descent. Either the game can stand up to multiple plays where the 'heroes' know the scenario, or it can't. If it can't then the game replay is shit, and if it can then how can it be balanced for both cases? With so much hidden info in Mansions the investigators hardly even know what they're doing, it's hard to be engaged in the game.

There are many very boring turns as an investigator. You often use your only (very precious) action on a turn to move again, in addition to your other 2. Getting around the map is tedious and unfulfilling. People talk about the great thematic flavor text and clues but as far as I can tell this is mostly hot air. You need to collect stuff from rooms, there is sometimes a useful 'hint' in the text as to which room to check, but you kind of want to check them all and the ones with locks are obviously more important.

The keeper seems to have lots of little decisions and interesting things to think about whereas the investigator turns are pretty much autoplay. In other games like this you could imagine trying to automate the keeper making the game full co-op (this is exactly what I do in Runebound Midnight), in this game I would rather be the keeper with automated investigators. One could play solo as the keeper. This lacks the balance that Descent has, in that game both sides have interesting things happening and it's almost inconceivable that either side could be automated. That balance is definitely missing here. This is another reason why the investigators should know what's in the keeper scenario book, it gives them some more decision space.

I would like to try this again sometime, but I also want to avoid it... so I don't know where that leaves me. Even if it's as good as I could imagine It would still be kind of long and not that great :P

RATING: 6
I bought Mansions of Madness, being hyped by al the attention it got shortly before and after its release. I've watched and read several reviews that pointed out a few flaws, and despite of that I got a copy. I really enjoy Arkham Horror, I love the artwork of the HP Lovecraft universe, and the theme is an amazing background for great storytelling. 
After playing through a couple of the scenarios as the Keeper, I felt that I really enjoyed the game, especially the way the storyline is interwoven throughout the game. Characters are simple enough to be easily playable, but provide enough variety to really matter in certain situations. Combat seems to work just fine. However, this game tends to last too long for what it delivers, and I'm not sure I like the stories that much. They seem too straightforward sometimes. I'm hoping that there will be a great expansion waiting for us, and I definately should try to play this game as one of the investigators.

RATING: 7
FFG succeeded in creating another bottomless money pit. Even after only two games with a friend's copy I can feel the urge of possessing the full monty with all expansions. And that's before the first of many expansions is on the market! If you are into Ameritrash, Mansions of Madness is all you could ask for. There's a great Cthulhu theme implemented in a shorter and less fiddly way than Arkham Horror. It's semi-cooperative and tells a story in an RPG-like fashion notwithstanding the limitations of boardgaming, i.e. having to stay within a defined frame. The typical risk of differing quality of story development is there but our second game had such a great story arc that I can live with it. Best of all, there's actual decisions to be made on both sides that make Mansions worth playing even for Eurogamers like me that were bored to death by the irrelevant gameplay of Last Night on Earth.
Nonetheless, I'm a bit hesitant when it comes to purchasing MoM on my own. A big part of the fun is discovering new rooms, monsters or spells. As it is, the fun will be more in new combinations of known components. Playing it with friends owning the game and moderating the scenarios might be the perfect way for this game.

RATING: 7.1
Number of players: 2-5

Components: (10)
This game looks wonderful! All the minis look so great! I would have liked if they had came painted, but that would have made the game so much more expensive. Also, I know that many people like that they come unpainted so that they can customize them in that way. They're all so distinct, however, that no one should ever have a problem confusing any of them with any other.
  
Rules: (7)
To be fair, I don't remember reading the rules to this game very well. I do know that it wasn't the most well written rule book. Fantasy Flight Games seem to excel in EVERYTHING they do, save for writing rules, at which they usually prove to be the worst in the industry. 

Setup: (4)
Don't get me wrong, here. Set up in worth every bit of hassle it takes, but it takes a lot. If you screw anything up, you'll screw the entire game up. Be careful. It's horrible.

Area required to play: (6)
The game will take up a fair amount of room, but it's really nice to see it set up.
 
Fun: (10)
And where it counts, this game delivers!

Theme Integration / Mechanics: (8)
Most of thing game is so great at to make it worth all the small things that I don't like about it, including some of the mechanics (the ones, really, that I was most excited about). Some of the puzzles and other things that make set-up so bad end up being really easy. It's still fun, though.

Replay Value: (7)
I love it and want to play it again all the time. However, it's so fiddly that you'll think twice before pulling it from the shelf.

Storage: (3)
Everything will fit back in the box somehow, but don't think for a minute that it'll be pretty or make any kind of sense.

Accessibility: (6)
It's not the worst game to introduce someone to, but it's not the easiest either. If they'll sit still for it, I think most anyone would have fun.

Over-All/Bonuses/Biases: (+10) All in all, I still really love this game. It's just too fiddly to make it to the table as often as it would otherwise.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I appreciate what this game is meant to do.  I think it fulfills its purpose wholeheartedly.  The problem is that I am simply not the target demographic for games like this.  I find the theme bordering on offensive.  I generally don't care for any type of fantasy or horror theme, but I'm not a big fan of the artwork or the occult themes that run throughout the game.

That said, they did a great job making the play time manageable, the ruleset fairly clean and the combat easily resolved.  This feels a ton more approachable than Arkham Horror, though I feel like these games have very little in common.  Mansions of Madness is essentially an exploration game, and while the puzzles are a unique addition, it is kind of stupid to have one person working with a puzzle while everyone else waits.

Aficionados of the game would seem to have a valid point if it seems like replayability is lacking (I'm sure there are plenty of expansions in the pipeline), though since I don't see myself playing it that much that is not a big deal for me.

Basically it comes down to the fact that FFG products admirably strive to create a certain ambiance that envelops you while playing.  I would probably appreciate this more if I was a fan of fantasy/scifi/horror themes in the first place.  But as it is I will play this a couple more times, but it would never be my first choice.

Can´t really say if he was listening, but he certainly went and made that nifty hand sized reference sheet...Check it out in the FILES section.

-Puzzles work great, but sometimes you´re very lucky with the pieces drawn, and they´re ridicously easy.
-Love it, already dreaming about what they´ll put in the expansions... and we all know that there WILL be expansions...

RATING: 9
This game definitely belongs in my collection, and I'm glad I splurged on it.  From the few times I've played Descent, my opinion is that they are very much the same.  There are a few differences, primarily in the "loot", because there are no chests in MoM, so what you start with will likely be what you use for the rest of the game, so choose your starting items wisely and try to balance the team.  Items exist in the game, but there is no surefire way to get them.  Something my group mentioned at least.  I've only played it twice but I LOVE the theme and the stories appear to be well developed.  We played the first scenario twice and I played keeper both times, but I really liked the way the decisions at the beginning weren't just numbers and setup decisions, but they had flavor text that told you what your decision meant thematically.

RATING: 6
CRITIQUE OF MANSIONS OF MADNESS
I don't own Mansions of Madness, and I haven't played it as the Keeper, so this critique is given simply based on several experiences playing a character in the game.

MoM is a "GM vs. Players" game with a strong Lovecraftian theme. It is highly thematic in addition to being strongly story driven. In fact, I'd say that MoM does a better job of being story driven than Mice & Mystics does, and M&M is all about story and theme.

I'm not a fan of cooperative games, because I find that they are too luck-driven. Cooperative games set you up against a random set of events going off and if you play it well the only thing that determines your success of failure is the order in which the events come up. But MoM actually pits the players against a live opponent, who actively sets up the obstacles and controls the creatures as they attempt to destroy the players. This is fantastic, and it is incredibly enjoyable to watch the Keeper try to make your fail your mission. This is the thing I like the most about MoM.

Another thing I think is very well done here are the various "tests" the characters will face in every scenario. They have some control over their ability scores and items, and they have a few skill points they can use to mitigate the luck of the die every game. But the Keeper will also be playing cards on them that impact their chances, and combat might have you rolling your Luck to hit, when you thought you'd be rolling your Marksmanship. Nice.

There are many things to like here: the great components, the interesting characters, the ready availabity of expansions, and of course (if you are into Lovecraft) the well-developed theme.

Unfortunately, the game also has the same problem that cooperative games share: how the Keeper's decks in MoM is going to play a major role in the success or failure of the investigators. It can't be helped. So, regardless of how effective they are, the investigators could either breeze through a scenario or get stomped, depending upon the luck of the draw. For a game that gets almost everything right, this is like a slap in the face.

MoM is a game that has gone on and off my wishlist, and is likely to stay off, at this point. There is a lot of tension in the game, but the players are mostly railroaded from one fairly obvious clue to the next, after bypassing a series of obstacles, the danger of which is up to random chance. In spite of this complaint, of all the Cthulhu mythos games I have played, MoM is by far the best. If you are a fan of Lovecraft, then I'd say MoM is probably a "must buy" game, and one that you will enjoy.

RATING: 5
I really WANT to love this game, but I can't. It has too many flaws like the permanent dull interruptions by keeper cards and the combat system that is pseudo-stylish but not very efficient and intuitive. 

Your gangster has a good markmanship skill? Well, when you shoot you always have the "luck" that the combat card tells you to test your low dexternity skill 'cause you are falling into the room instead of simply walking and firing. So, you don't hit anything. Wow...

But the main problem is the card "Uncontrollable Urges". It's not broken, but it is getting on the nerves very quickly. It feels for the investigators as if they were a group of old senile seniors who can't hold an item, shoot on friends and can't walk a hallway without loosing a shoe. It's very annoying when one investigator always looses a key item because the keeper tells him to do so. And you always have to invest an action (the one per game round you have...) to get the item back into the hands. And when a friend approaches to help you, your character will shoot at him with his gun.

Without this dumb card the game could be very cool. And we always hope for a good story that helps forget these annoying flaws... It IS a fun and nice looking game and we will play it from time to time, but "Arkham Horror" is much better for getting into the Mythos.

RATING: 6
The combat system has probably the best simplicity vs. interesting-range-of-results I've seen in any game.

Although the setup is a little time-consuming (and easy for the keeper to screw up in game-ruining ways), I like the story-influenced arrangement better than Betrayal at House on the Hill or Descent or Arkham Horror.  I also like that it has simpler rules than Descent or Arkham Horror, and that player turns are faster than Descent.

The burrito boy (now 11) said he felt the game didn't have much strategy, which was an interesting observation; I think he may be right.  Maybe we'll feel differently after a couple more plays.

I may try running this one week, and a one-shot Call of Cthulhu game the next week, and see which the crowd prefers.  I kind of think the RPG will win.  (I run really rules-light, so I expect [i]less[/i] of a rules burden on the players in the RPG than in the board game.)  The disadvantages of the RPG are more pre-game prep time for the keeper, and no replayability of a given scenario, but in exchange you get increased flexibility, the elimination of rules nonsense--"but I have the Lantern, so why am I affected by the Power Failure lock?"--and the elimination of the inherent lameness of trying to represent a Lovecraftian horror with a plastic figure.

[b]Update:[/b] the first play with the regular group [article=6427357]didn't go so well[/article].  This game has one or two more chances to deliver some crazy fun.  [i]You hear me, Mansions of Madness?  I'll trade-pile your ass![/i]  The story-influenced arrangement I was excited about doesn't really deliver, as there's no connection between the story choices; you might as well pick your objective & story choice tiles at random.

[b]Update:[/b] wasn't getting played; gave to Darin 2012-03-01.

RATING: 6.5
Wow! This game is really something. If you want to scratch the Arkham itch in less time and with more of a storytelling aspect, this is the way to go.

Unfortunately, not all scenarios are created equal. The game is pretty unbalanced in favor of the keeper, and some characters have either far too little sanity or far too little stamina: one wrong character pick could end in disaster.

Additionally, there have been reported cases where one side could set up an automatic win from the get-go, or where some objectives are stupidly unfair. And after a few plays, the battle cards get repetitive and even inane. But with a little tweaking this game could be a classic.

RATING: N/A
Mansions of Madness is a macabre game of horror, insanity, and mystery for two to five players. Each game takes place within a pre-designed story that provides players with a unique map and several combinations of plot threads. These threads affect the monsters that investigators may encounter, the clues they need to find, and which climatic story ending they will ultimately experience. One player takes on the role of the keeper, controlling the monsters and other malicious powers within the story. The other players take on the role of investigators, searching for answers while struggling to survive with their minds intact.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
► Best atmospheric game.

[i]Note: This is by far my most subjective rating, so I am absolutely aware that people who don't care for the game's genre and Lovecraftian theme will rank this game lower.[/i]

[b]Mansions of Madness[/b] is without doubt flawed in its details (very long setup, puzzles slow down the game's flow way too much, the game's balance favours the Keeper), but the positive aspects easily outweight the negative ones for me personally (fantastic atmosphere, modular stories and board pieces for a better replay value, stunning component quality). It's essentially a roleplaying game in a box.

I would rank this game a 10/10, if said flaws would not exist in this game - so it's "just" a very high 9.

Because of the game's balance issues, the player who takes on the role as the evil Keeper should not play this game too competitively, but understand his/her role more as a story-teller like in a classic Pen & Paper roleplaying game - but it's a balancing act which is hard to pull off and requires a lot of experience with all the scenarios and story milestone choices.

(Rating based on the base game.)

RATING: 5
This is a bit of a blend of Betrayal House on Haunted Hill and Arkham Horror.  In the end, I'd just prefer to play Betrayal.

LIKES:

* Game play is fairly straightforward but the game does have an extensive rule-set.  Not as bad as Arkham though!
* The miniatures are actually quite impressive...the larger monsters are quite fearsome!
* Game play for an investigator is quite simple.  The monster player has quite a bit more going on...

DISLIKES:

* I prefer my adventure/story telling games to be quick rule instruction and play.  While this one isn't nearly as complex as Arkham, there are enough moving parts to make this one slow down as you check a rule.
* Puzzles were an interesting twist, but they are quite simple.  Seems pointless outside of another way to make investigators waste/spend time and/or clue tokens.  
* Some gameplay doesn't make sense.  Apparently I am suddenly struck with terror/horror when I see a zombie that I passed a horror check on earlier and know that he walked out that door but it still shocks me.  Yeah, whatever.  
* I realize there are a couple modifiers available, but I don't really like so much resting on a 10 sided die.  Most of the time that means us investigators are playing somewhere between 40-60% odds on dice rolls.  
* Not a fan of scenarios where neither the DM nor the investigators win.  So, we just played for 3-4 hours so no one could win?  What a bunch of crock!

RATING: 7
This game has so much promise but is hamstrung by several factors.
* The players have to be really into the theme and style of game play or they will be miserable.
* Several games have ended abruptly from the event cards that left a bitter taste.
* There are only five scenarios, despite the variation within each.
* Some scenarios have very little monster interaction, I feel like the multitude of miniatures supplied isn't really warranted.
* Some of the mechanics are clumsy, such as an investigator wasting a whole action in their turn to drop or pickup an item.
* The volume of errors and errata is disappointing for what I would consider a premium game.
The game has many strong points but these aspects detract from what could have been amazing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mansions is Betrayal at House on the Hill done even better.

I'm personally a fan of Betrayal but have some caveats about the game. Mansions not only applies a theme/universe that I am passionate about but amps up the component quality while reducing the fiddlyness of the entire experience. 

I'm so glad the game intentionally splits setup between the players and the keeps. This reduces front load down time by a large margin. It immediately involves all players. Another element that does this is the way the keep stays the moderator for all cards and puzzles that are revealed, and further the mythos cards that have to be played on the investigators turn; making the keeper highly interested in what the investigators are doing on their turn. 

Turns go by fast generally. The play time is listed as 3-4 hours and our learning game with numerous interruptions was 4. I can easily see this game being a 2 hour experience with our group, which is simply awesome.

I'm not sure how necessary the monster figs are, as they are gigantic, but I simply wouldn't have the game any other way.

There are complaints about two other things:

Errata: Yep. All FF has it. I don't have an issue with it. Generally if we cannot find the answer here we all agree on one until we find out the "correct" answer.

Story Telling: There is a story here. It's all theme. No it doesn't read like a book, or even play out like a movie, but it does serve to get players involved and they are interested to hear what comes next. The story does get lost while your playing until that next chapter comes up again.

I'm looking forward to expansions for this one in a big way and indeed I also see this rising to a 10.

Update:

Rating lowered to 7 when compared to the new edition. There's no going back to this, and for us that is amazing and surprising.

RATING: 9
[microbadge=419][microbadge=5792][microbadge=2135]

If Corey Konieczka had a mandate to re-implement the Descent dungeon-crawl system, he knocked it out of the park.  Moving a dungeon-crawl into a haunted house is a little ingenious and turns out to be highly effective.  The game has a great deal of complexity in its interlocking systems, but the complexity turns out to be almost entirely in the scenario design.  Once the player has actually arrived at the game, as either an investigator or as the keeper, the complexity is almost entirely gone, replaced by a streamlined and fast-paced experience.  Combat is more rare than in Descent or Arkham Horror, but also more brutal and deadly.  The puzzle system is even more fun than it initially looked.  But it is the clue-driven storyline that makes this game a real winner.

Steffi
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After a couple of plays of Arkham Horror, I commented that that game would have worked better if it weren't a pure co-op, but rather had one player controlling the enemies. I'm therefore glad to see Mansions of Madness, which takes the Arkham Horror theme (Cthulhu Mythos) and puts it in a smaller scale setting.

Having one player act as the "Keeper" achieves a couple of incredible things for gameplay. Firstly, there's not as much confusing housekeeping following the scripted rules for monster movement, et cetera, which makes for smoother gameplay. Then there's the fact that your enemies are controlled by an intelligent player, which not only makes for a more authentic challenge (as opposed to being defeated by unfortunate card draws), it also gives the conflict more meaning. Lastly, the Keeper gets secret objectives, so there's an added element of suspense which makes the game more exciting.

There are some downsides to this game. The rulebook, as I've come to expect from games in this genre, isn't always set out as well as it could be, and after a couple of games we're still discovering things we've been doing wrong. Set-up time is considerable, in large part because the Keeper needs to do quite a bit of set-up in secret. These are minor quibbles though.

This game is great fun. The artwork is excellent. The inclusion of puzzles is innovative, and brilliantly executed. The stories are interesting enough to make you care, and the use of pre-written scenarios gives the game a rhythm and pacing that random-card-draw co-ops never have.

RATING: 8
While this game is not nearly as good as arkham horror i really love the game experience!

Some comments on what others have said about the game.

1.) Yes, the setup takes some time. But as you get used to the game mechanics you can do it in about 30 minutes which is a lot shorter than sorting all the cards out of the expansions of arkham horror.

2.) The game gets a lot better with experienced players and keeper. When you know the rules well and have some kind of strategy on how to use both your monsters, threats and trauma (as keeper) or your characters as investigator the gaming experience significantly improves.

3.) Replayability. Well this is a tough one to defend. I have only played the game six times. Three as an investigator and three as keeper. And it does seem that once you have played a scenario you basically know what to do the next time you play it. But the game is still a lot of fun though! And hopefully we'll get loads and loads of "fan written" scenarios to keep the game interesting. And if not, fantasy flight seems to love expansions. 

In conclusion, the game has its flaws. There's no denying that! But i'll probably be playing this game in years from now. As long as there are some new scenarios out there. But i would guess that you will get tired of playing the same scenarios too many times since there are not enough variation in them to keep the game interesting after too many replays.

RATING: N/A
Horrific monsters and spectral presences lurk in manors, crypts, schools, monasteries, and derelict buildings near Arkham, Massachusetts. Some spin dark conspiracies while others wait for hapless victims to devour or drive insane. It’s up to a handful of brave investigators to explore these cursed places and uncover the truth about the living nightmares within.
Designed by Corey Konieczka, Mansions of Madness is a macabre game of horror, insanity, and mystery for two to five players. Each game takes place within a pre-designed story that provides players with a unique map and several combinations of plot threads. These threads affect the monsters that investigators may encounter, the clues they need to find, and which climactic story ending they will ultimately experience. One player takes on the role of the keeper, controlling the monsters and other malicious powers within the story. The other players take on the role of investigators, searching for answers while struggling to survive with their minds intact.
Do you dare enter the Mansions of Madness?

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Aaand they're doing it again, after Runewars and Civilizations! Right now, my biggest complaint about FFG is that they are churning out their excellent, groundbreaking big games too fast for me to get enough play out of them.

Mansions would have been almost an automatic buy just for the toy value, especially since we're occasionally playing the Call of Cthulhu RPG (and I have tentative hopes for a Trail of Cthulhu session). But it is a solid game, excellent in places (the trauma cards and puzzles are high design points, I feel) and with a totally and overwhelmingly brilliant way of handling scenario setup: 

The keeper just decides on a couple of story points (like where the adversary is keeping the corpses, and what exactly the big secret behind all these mysterious going-ons is) and the game takes over from there. This leads to a very unique game experience where the first run of a scenario is a very story-driven affair for both the investigators and the keeper, since neither knows exactly what the story events will be, whereas subsequent plays get more and more strategic on the investigators' side and heavy on the bluffing from the keeper.

Even if you want to restrict yourself to the adventurous and atmospheric side of the Mansions of Madness package, you should get at least three plays out of each scenario, which means a total of fifteen plays for the base game - whether this is insufficient or quite a lot depends on your buying and gaming habits, I guess. Me, I've just enjoyed the fifth replay of the first scenario immensely, so I'm sure I got my money's worth.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
We need to talk about MoM.

The Good: It's a scenario-based tactical game of Lovecraftian horror with the usual FFG polish, a rich narrative, and brilliant gameplay that requires a careful attention to the story.

The Bad: Some scenarios are programmed brokenly, are of varying quality, and just plain don't work. Additionally, replayability is so-so once you've figured out what the key to each scenario is.

The Ugly: Set up time is atrocious, and misplacing even one card during the setup can render the game unwinnable by the investigators.

So, MoM: It's a mixed bag for sure, but it's still one of my favorites. You have to love the theme to get the most enjoyment out of it, and you have to be willing to have lost without entirely knowing why.

RATING: 5
I love this type of game (beautiful components, lots of fluff, not too abstract). The similarities with Descent are obvious and this is a good thing (I really like Descent). Some sessions have been exciting and close, sometimes not decided until the last roll on the last turn. Some scenarios are really problematic and can lead to very short games or be downright boring. Not sure if this type of game can be properly balanced for competitive play (I don't think that is a problem though, just something you need to be aware). After a few more plays I think the game is not tense enough and this is mainly due to fiddlyness and the problematic scenarios. There are also a lot of misprints and small rules issues that detract from the game.

RATING: 6.5
Thoughts up to scenario 3.  I've only been an investigator.

What I like:
- Background story and flavor text is entertaining
- It's a fun experience.  We try to immerse ourselves and imagine one of those cheesy horror flicks.
- So far the scenarios seem relatively balanced as both the investigators and keepers are inexperienced.  Except for scenario 3, which only seems balanced if the investigators are already familiar with the objectives.
- Monsters don't block paths. You just have to evade them.
- You can spend skill points to mitigate the dice rolls

What I don't like:
- It's long.  I don't know what we're doing wrong. Scenario 1 took us 4 hours and scenario 2 took us 6 hours to finish.
- The interruption of the investgators with the mythos and trauma cards is annoying as an investigator, but I guess it is probably needed to give the keeper the ability to slow down the investigators
- The respawning of the investigators seems weird but I can't imagine the investigators ever winning without that rule.
- It's hard to heal damage/horror
- Dice seem to hate me
- Replayability? Much of the appeal of the investigator's role seems to be discovering the objective.

RATING: 9
When I was a little kiddo I enjoyed making papercrafts.
One day on a comic I found one called "the haunted house".
Till this day (20 years after) I still have it.
A four stores building with a spooky garden entrance, that is split in half on the back, so you that can see each room.Full of monsters and traps(they could actually move),it was a perfect set for me and my cousin to play with.
I putted a message on the front door and from that point after he was running through those haunted corridors,trying to avoid any encounters (rarely)and find the next clue I've left for him.
And suddenly,all those years after,Mansions arrives in our hands.
Being both of us,lovers of horror and especially of H.P.Lovecraft,words are very poor to describe what we feel for this game and the hours we spend with it!

RATING: 5.5
The perfect specimen of an ameritrash game with a deep theme and loads of fiddly rules that tries to accommodate it. It is essentially a Lovecraftian-themed version of Clue with some combat thrown in. Clue might offer more fun, though.

Restrictive in terms of actual choises for players and rules that makes little actual sense (why can you see the same cultist over and over and get scared every time you pop your head in the doorway until you are driven insane, yet happily remain standing whistling in the same room as a Shoggoth if you want to for turns over until he deigns devouring you?). Too reliant on luck--there are dice rolls for everything. Incongruent game mechanics with jigsaw-like puzzles thrown at the players now and then. Set-up possibly encouraging playing the dungeon keeper sadisticly, which can be detrimental to the gaming atmosphere. It is a board game that by its theme and heavy emphasis on story telling and character back stories suggests to be more that in is but fails to live up to that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A beautifully designed game with high quality components. Thematically, the game is excellent. The countdown mechanics of sanity, health and the event deck could go a long way to create the sense of tension one would expect from a Cthulhu themed game but it fails to deliver that tension by interrupting it with instant "you lose" style mechanics. An unrealized horror is merely a surprise but the horror you see coming and cannot stop is truly terrifying. Ultimately the players are left unsatisfied because story isn't explained in any meaningful way to the players once the game is over.

A friend sums it up nicely, "Try spending half an hour setting up the complex “Green-Eyed Boy” scenario and then have the players lose the game in round 4. It’s jarring and doesn't endear gamers who are expecting a boardgame only to discover they're playing a "Choose Your Own Adventure" book with miniatures."

RATING: 9
Ich liebe Villen des Wahnsinns, kann aber verstehen, dass es einige Spieler gibt, die mit so einem Spiel nicht viel anfangen können. Denn in Villen des Wahnsinns, muss man sich auf die Atmosphäre und die Geschichte einlassen können, da hier die ganz großen Stärken des Spiels sind. In Villen des Wahnsinns spielt ein Spieler den böse Bewahrer und die anderen Spieler die heldenhaften Ermittler, die versuchen einem mysteriösen Fall aufzuklären. Das Spiel liefert unterschiedliche Szenarien, die der Bewahrer durch bestimmte Entscheidungen vor Spielbeginn variieren kann. So entsteht ein immer neues Spielerlebnis. Anders als bei z.B. Descent liegt der Fokus bei Villen des Wahnsinns nicht auf Kampf, sondern auf Detektivarbeit, Rätsel lösen und Erforschung. Fast alle Karten kommen mit einem stimmigen Flavortext daher und meine dringende Empfehlung ist es, diese auch zu beachten. Eine stimmungsvolle musikalische Untermalung (bei uns läuft immer der Soundtrack von Resident Evil), hilft die gruselige und beklemmende Atmosphäre zu unterstreichen. Die regeln sind durchaus komplex und der Bewahrer sollte von einem erfahrenen Spieler gespielt werden. Dann aber erwartet Euch mitVillen des Wahnsinns, ein außergewöhnliches Erlebnis!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The Arkham Horror equivalent to Descent (1st Ed.), Mansions of Madness is very easy on the eye but very, very fiddly and at times overly complex.

As host (and most probably Keeper) I often find I'm  mentally exhausted after playing! There's so many fiddly rules to remember and enforce, and then I'm trying to plan my own turn and card plays on top!

MoM's unique narrative-strong scenarios also take a great amount of preparation and set-up before play. I often go through everything prior to the night, set-up the map, test the seeding to make sure there are no errors, collect together all the appropriate cards, monsters, puzzles and tokens, check through the scenario for what to expect, check online for FAQs to problems... Up to a good hour of preparation is in store for the poor Keeper! 

The miniatures and general aesthetics of the game are incredible. This is sobered a bit by an incredible asking price and what appears to be a surprising lack of gaming content considering the amount of stuff you get in the box. 

I think that FFG's boasts to MoM having lots of re-playability is a tall statement as a base game- when it comes down to it there's only five scenarios, all of which repeat maps and events with its limited set of cards and monsters. There's piles of scenario expansions to counter this problem, but this also makes it a hellish money sink too.

MoM is also well known for being peppered with errors, mistakes and bugs that often weigh the game heavily in the favour of one side or spoil the whole thing altogether! The Scenario 'Green Eyed Boy' is the worst offender, and is so bad even the map is set up incorrectly in two places! If you didn't know about this in advance you'd be screwed and the lengthy set-up would be for nought!

Sometimes it feels like there should be more happening as well- in the larger scenarios there is often very little occurring. Monsters are actually quite rare and pending specific Keeper Action cards some scenarios are lucky to see more then 2 monsters all game!

Its also quite finicky at times. Combat is exciting and dynamic, but because it uses decks of cards and you must cycle through to the next appropriate weapon type when an attack is made you often find every declaration of combat involves discarding through half the deck before you find a relevant card! I found that 'unarmed' is the most common attack my investigators make but there's only about 4 of these cards meaning you see each one 15 times in each game!

I like the Resident Evil style puzzles but they brake the game up quite badly and are awkward and inappropriate considering the rest of the game's pace. 

I quite enjoy the experience of MoM when it works, but I'm glad I only paid half the price for the game (got it second hand). When its good though its GREAT. When its bad its almost unplayable!

I've applied several patches, including corrected scenarios for the base game, card clarifications and changes to many of the cards and stories for balance and better play. Sadly my group still seems a little disappointed, mainly because they haven't grasped that the tone of the game is 'explore, uncover plot and avoid monsters' rather then 'kill all monsters and do that story thing if we have time afterwards'..

Progressive Rating: 9-8-7-8-7.

RATING: 7.5
Reminds me of a blend between Arkham Horror, Middle Earth Quest and Betrayal at House on the Hill with a hint of Descent. This is not surprising given that those games are all within the FFG stable. As with those games, I would be careful to suggest this game to europurists because Mansions of Madness gets its fun from the willingness of the participants to get into the theme. 

From one play of Mansions of Madness I thought it was a fun, well-produced game dripping in its theme as is typical of FFG games. It moves at a quick pace with little downtime but it did seem to feel a bit dull somewhere around the middle and didn't quite have that nail-biting denounement that the other games I mentioned above seemed to have. This might improve on further plays and greater familiarity with the rules, which should speed up resolution of the game. 

Would definitely play this one again if offered. I think downtime might be an issue with more than 3 investigators, though and is probably best with just 2 (ie a 3 player game).

RATING: 8.8
One of best new games I've played in years and only game I've ever played 4 or 5 times the first week I've owned it.

BRILLIANT F##$@ing ADVICE: WHEN YOU PLAY THIS GAME: play it using the soundtrack to the computer game "Amnesia the Dark Descent" TRUST ME IT WORKS PERFECTLY. Make your own soundtrack by downloading all the tracks from youtube (provided you have permission from the publisher or whatever or purchase soundtrack)

THEN: put the tracks together making it so that most tracks (since they're made to loop and are therefore short) have the same track over and over again and build them up in order from most relaxed to most tense. So your CD will look like this

SONG 1
SONG 1 
SONG 1
SONG 2
SONG 2
SONG 2
Etc. This makes each track long enough that the CD is long enough and there's not too much abrupt shifting of moods. 

ANother tip as you play the game occaisionally rewind the CD a few tracks so that the epic finale in the game synchs up with the LAST track on the CD which I recommend you make "Daniel's Ending" works perfect to give a GREAT LOVECRAFTIAN feel throughout your entire game and makes a beautiful board game that's dripping in theme THAT MUCH MORE. Sorry for all the caps, just got too excited thinking about that and all the fun we've had doing this. It's worth the effort.

Best game of it's type, they can not make expansions and new content fast enough. Brilliant replayability despite the haters just within the base game too! BUY IT!

UPDATE: still a great game. Use the woman in black soundtrack for this game as well. Works well.

RATING: 3
I really wanted this one to work out – it seemed like a great approach to the whole Cthulhu thing, having the players actually play through a genuine prefabricated story, trying to unravel the goal that their enemy is constantly working towards. But it just doesn't work. 

The mechanics of the game don't help – there's nothing really inspired in the design here – but the real problem is that the actual plots make no sense at all, which just destroys the premise of the game. 

It was when I found myself spending lots of time trying to prepare for playing this game by trying to make the plots make some kind of sense, and changing flavour text to reflect this, that I realised just how badly this game was ripping me off. THE GAME IS BASED ON THESE PLOTS – HOW HARD IS IT TO MAKE THEM MAKE SENSE?

The whole thing just has the feel of being rushed to publication well before it should have been.

RATING: 6.5
The closest incarnation to the vision of storytelling boardgames FFG's been striving to attain... and I wish I liked it more.

I don't dislike it.  There's a decent game here.  But it's a role-playing game.  It's moved from a boardgame to a RPG in much the same way that Descent slowly moved to a primarily RPG experience.  It just arrived there, fully born, from conception.  And again, that's fine.

But here's the thing: There's a Call of Chthulu RPG out there.  It's one of the best RPGs ever made.  And Trail of Cthulhu kicks ITS ass by eliminating CoC's biggest issue.  It's your primary competition, not Arkham Horror.  And no offense to your lovely figurines, your very warped boards, or your 900 little cards, but it kicks your ass.  Don't worry.  It kicks MOST games' collective ass.  It's an unfair competition, but that's what Lovecraft was all about: a lopsided, uncaring universe built to destroy us by design and the only thing keeping humanity sane is its relative ignorance.

What you've created is the CoC equivalent of a 4th Ed. D&D world.  Fair enough.  And if it's alright with y'all, I'll just play Trail of Cthulhu.

RATING: 7
First play was more crazy slapstick than horror. It felt like an early film by Peter Jackson than a gothic novel. I've rarely heard more people laughing out loudly when playing a board game. But no doubt, the game is very thematic and entertaining for the investigators in any case. The keeper, however, has got a tougher job. He's the one who absolutely needs to know the rules. And they are many, though they're not complicated. It's one of the most story telling games I have played in my life. How much would I want to play the game right now? Any time, as long a I don't have to play the keeper. But it's not the kind of game I would want to build up or explain to others. 

Also, people say that many of the scenarios are either buggy and/or unbalanced; of course, the game is about the theme and not the winning, but games without tension, games without a dramatic curve aiming at a close ending aren't entertaining either. I haven't played one of the unbalanced scenarios yet, but their undisputed existence is one more reason why I'll usually be willing to join the game, but never own it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
This is a tough game to give a review. This is a great game! If you are a horror game fan, then you really don't want to miss this. If you are a fan of Betrayal at House on the Hill, then again you don't want to miss this. In fact, this is a great 2-player game. If you have played games like Fury of Dracula (Dracula player), Scotland Yard (Mr. X), or Pandemic On the Brink (Bio-terrorist), where one player is against all others, then this is a similar experience. The components are excellent quality and the artwork is absolutely perfect. The box art is absolutely hands down irresistible. The stories are excellent and the mechanics of setting up each game are unique and actually quite fun. The game is really very simple to play. The rules are easy to teach and relearn. If you play as the Keeper, you can preset up the game and any new gamer could jump right in and play, with very little rules explanation. The game can be long, even though the actions are very simple for all players. Puzzles and other fun effects that happen in the game tend to make this game last longer than you expect, but not necessarily in a bad way. This game is also very balanced between the investigators and the Keeper...very enjoyable for both sides to play.

After playing my first game, I had a great time playing but was so disappointed that with only a set of 6 stories included in the game...this was just way too little. I worried also that replay ability would be quite bad. But here is what I have discovered so far. Each story has a set of questions allowing the Keeper to set up each game differently. There are at least 3 significant permutations of each story, I believe; this should allow for some variety of game play among the stories also. If you get on a kick of playing Mansions of Madness, most likely you will not play all of the stories back to back or probably won't want to play a story more than one time in a row. Even if you have a game group that wants to play this game often, I think it is possible that the locations of the most significant cards and clues could be memorized or at least anticipated to be in certain possible rooms, but I do not feel it will ruin the experience or even give away too much information. I also think that if the game were to sit on your shelf for awhile and you were to return to it in say 6 months, I would say you could replay all of the stories again as a fresh experience. 

For example: We played Blood Ties one evening with myself as Keeper, a couple of nights later played the Til Death Do Us Part expansion and about 1 week later played Blood Ties again with a new player as Keeper. His choices for the clues were different from mine, creating a very different story. Having been Keeper just a little while ago, I did have some knowledge of where possible clues might be. But here's the thing, when you look over the board you see where locked rooms and obstacles are, but if it is slightly varied you still have no idea which way to go. There was a point where I felt the final clue could possibly be in one of 3 possible rooms, but it was a tough decision for me, to which of those rooms I should concentrate my efforts. The other thing that occurred between the 2 blood ties games, was the stories created a different effect with monsters...for me that made the whole game totally different. I thought I went into the game knowing too much, but it turned out it didn't matter at all. Playing Blood Ties, then a different story and then coming back to Blood Ties, has a visual effect on you too. After playing just one different story between the 2 Blood Ties games, it really disorients you, because of the complete rearranging of the map tiles.

I cannot fault Fantasy Flight for producing such a game, as others have. This is a great game and I never could have guessed the replay ability would be this good. I would truly miss not being able to play this. I am very thankful that such a game is available, it is a great spooky romp through a haunted mansion...a theme of which I can never seem to get enough. I now, actually feel that I cannot see a point where I will have exhausted all of the variable options for all of the stories. I really have a blast playing MoM.

I have now purchased 4 mini expansions and the Alchemy expansion for even more replay ability. Not because I had too, but because it is that good.

RATING: 6
Our chief weapon is the theme. The theme and the whacking. Our two weapons are the whacking and the theme ... and ruthless insanity. Our three weapons are the whackings, the theme and ruthless insanity ... and an almost fanatical devotion to the die. Before you die. Scenarios are the typical Arkham lot ... wander around a mansion, unlock doors, find stuff which will unlock other doors, whack an endless supply of monsters that will delay you while each encounter drops your health and sanity. Which allows the Director aka Malevelant God to pour more pain on you via cards (mainly by dropping your ability to pass die tests). The malicious God can have some fun as he knows what's going on and can pour it on. Turns for the rest are ultra decision light - move and/or attack, see what happens, roll a die to resolve. So it's all about the theme. Each scenario has lots of variable setup, which contributes to a *long* setup time for the Director, but allows you to play each time facing a new challenge. If you're happy to play for the ride, and happy for someone to Direct, and don't care too much what happens or that it's decision-light ... you can have a fun time. The problem for the rest of us that while the Director has lots to decide and do (creating downtime for the rest), the game goes waaaay too long when you have no interesting decisions and where you're continually waiting through the downtime for the Director to choose how to pound you. And all that variability on promise is a disguise for doing much the same things every scenario ... wander to one end of the mansion, find something, go to the other end and use it, then back again, rolling dice to resolve attacks along the way. I was fine when my first game took 2 hours. It outstayed its welcome the second time and dropped rating points at 4.5 hours when it was clear the theme is too thin a veneer over lack of substance.

RATING: 4
"Decent core, but WAY too long (4.5 hrs), and I don't like the 1-vs-many structure."

What I like:
- Modular tile board is cool
- Narrative flavor is decent
- The fonts are slightly less unreadable than most FFG Lovecraft games

What I don't like:
- At 4.5 hours, it was about 3 hours too long
- The keeper seemed far too powerful
- It has about 70% of the complexity of Arkham Horror, which is far too much for a 2011 design

I didn't mind giving this a try, as I have moderately enjoyed Elder Sign and Eldritch Horror. I had no idea I was sacrificing over 5 hours of my life (including setup). For a game that I'm pretty sure would have ended with a keeper victory no matter what we would have done. When we lost (4.5 hours in), we were still *at least* 8 rounds from winning, and that would be if everything went perfectly. I can only hope that it would be shorter with fewer players, so I voted "not recommended" with 5p. 

I would be willing to try the newer edition that uses an app. Playing against a human took away a lot of what I enjoy in a co-op. 

I would rate the weight at 3.5, due to the many complicated little rules. I would give the depth about 1.7, on par with Forbidden Island.

RATING: 4
Mansions of Madness si presenta molto bene, con una confezione lussuosa, componenti ottimi e una grafica superba (al solito, la Fantasy Flight ha pochi rivali in questo).

Il tema è molto ben reso e alcuni meccanismi, come il puzzle per aprire le serrature, il sistema degli indizi e lo scenario, bene si prestano al gioco. Sembra di giocare a una versione migliorata di Betrayal at the house on the Hill.

Purtroppo dietro c'è la testa di Koniecza (quello che ha preso la briscola, l'ha complicata all'infinito e ci ha tirato fuori Battlestar Galactica) e la mano della FFG (mille pagine di regole? Ci sono. Mille pagine di errata? Eccole. Quattro chili di materiale di cui si poteva fare a meno? Come no?).

Il gioco è terribilmente lungo da preparare e terribilmente lento da giocare. La miglior rappresentazione del difetto dei giochi cooperativi: un gioco per due mascherato da 2-5 giocatori, dove gli investigatori cogitano a lungo per fare due passi e un'azione a testa ma giocano come un'unica entità, l'antagonista che impazzisce davanti alle scelte a disposizione.

Com'è possibile che un gioco basato pesantemente sull'elemento di  narrazione abbia tempi morti così lunghi (e poco più che una manciata di scenari)? Da una parte lo sforzo - meritorio, per carità - di allontanarsi dalle aride meccaniche tedesche privilegiando la trama e dall'altra le stesse iper-arzigogolate meccaniche, progettate SULLA trama, che generano tempi morti che ti obbligano a "uscire dalla storia" per pensare a ottimizzare bonus e decidere dove andrà il tuo compagno a quattro stanze di distanza. 

Meccaniche che, tra parentesi, sono per la maggior parte dei casi riconducibili al giocare una carta, scoprire una carta, tirare un dado, leggere una carta, riprendere in mano un carta. Il tutto inserito in un poco originale dungeoncrawl.

Un'altra idea interessante come le carte combattimento diventa un boomerang che fa a botte con il tema: ho una buona mira e un'arma, attacco uno zombie MA la carta mi obbliga a fare un check sulla mia destrezza, che magari fa schifo. Ma perché? E poi, la sequenza fissa di gioco che rende macchinoso il passaggio di oggetti da parte dell'ultimo giocatore, i personaggi con un disturbo alla memoria a breve, che fanno horror check su horror check 

Non hai fatto nessuno sforzo per le meccaniche (che di fatto non hanno niente di originale, si trovano in tutti i giochi americani), almeno sforzati di adattarle al tema, cribbio!

Troppe, troppe, TROPPE regole inutili, complicazioni e casi particolari inutili, carte e cartine INUTILI. Tre ore di gioco complessivo, tempo effettivo di gioco 45 minuti. 

Mansions of Madness è un Betrayal con le penosissime regole di Last night on Earth. Betrayal, con tutti i suoi limiti di regolamento e di qualità dei componenti, batte questo ennesimo briscolone della FFG sei a zero e dura un decimo. 

Per dirla come gli americani, tanto flavor, ma overcomplicated, disappointing & fiddly gameplay. Delusione cocente... il peggior gioco, finora, del 2011: ridatemi le semplici meccaniche tedesche, domani mattina mi devo alzare per andare a lavoro, non posso fare le 3.

RATING: 8
Only one solo play so far, and given that this is a decidedly multiplayer affair, I may not be in the best position to judge it's merits.  I did enjoy the one play I've had so far though and I can definitely see the potential for a fantastic game here.

The basic premise is a one against many game, where a team of players control investigators exploring a haunted house trying to uncover its secrets, whilst another player controls the evil forces present in the house in an attempt to kill or drive off the investigators.  The game is scenario based and comes with 5 scenarios each with multiple different branching paths which potentially makes each play through of a scenario slightly different.

Theme is king here and the game simply drips with it.  That said the gameplay is also solid but it's not going to burn your brain like Agricola for instance.

Have high hopes for this one, so hoping it gets some table time soon.

RATING: 8
The latest, and probably most successful effort at the "explore a haunted house" genre.

I come at MoM as an Arkham Horror fan, and although I can see where people find MoM more engaging, it's subject to greater fluctuations in luck and has enough differentiation in the learning curve (it's easier to "learn" to play the Keeper well than to "learn" to play the Investigators well) that I have, after two plays (one on each side), found it largely unsatisfying.

Its inherent advantages -- variability and flavor -- are undermined by the overall randomness and subjection to imbalance that so many possible scenarios have in a game where decision making, to be frank, is severely limited compared to those possible in an actual RPG.  The clue progression is ham-fistedly linear, though the game masks this fact by tempting the investigators into searching every room, not necessarily the ones the clues suggest.  But if the investigators do this, they tend to slow their progress so much that the Keeper can hammer them. The result (in my admittedly limited experience) is a game that seems to encourage open-ended decision making, but really doesn't.  (The room vs. space differences can also be a bit tricky in remembering some rules, and it's tedious to remember to do immediate horror and when evade checks are necessary.)

That said, many ratings of this game oppose my own opinion, and I've only played twice. The narrative is far stronger than in Arkham, the map and pieces are minis, not cardboard on stands, and the puzzles are a cool inclusion.

I'm trying to understand my preference for AH over MoM (a preference that makes me the outsider of our game group), and I think it's because I see more interesting decisions to make as an individual in AH, and its variations (though less flavorful) have a standard balance to them and allows for clever problem-solving (especially in a tougher game).  To wit, AH has "skill sliders", which make for far more interesting decision-making.

RATING: 2
Mansions of Madness takes the GM : players interactions of actual RPGs, most notably in a line of descent consisting of DnD to Descent, and for my own comparison, the further offspring that is Star Wars: Imperial Assault. However, this game also has to incorporate the clunky mechanisms of Arkham Horror, and its sister game (in that thematically they tread common horror ground) Betrayal at House on the Hill. As such, there's lots of incomplete information where your actual goal is to do the hidden information, along with just so rules and clunky setup and a general sense of futility. In an RPG about horror and lurking doom, this is great thematically, but mechanically you should't be bored waiting for your turn, and then be bored IN your turn.

The worst aspects of the rules from Betrayal were combined with the worst aspects of the feel of Imperial Assault, and then dressed up in the recycled art assets of the Fantasy Flight properties. Almost disgusting, especially as it ends up being less than the sum of its parts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Only two words: PURE ATMOSPHERE. I can say without doubt that I was expecting a game like this one since years!
It is a mix of Betrayal at House on the Hill, Descent and Arkham Horror, but it merges the best of all of them in my opinion, with an astounding mix of flavor and original yet simple mechanics. The scenarios' structure is simply great and the way the game plays ensure also a good replayability, better than I was expecting - there are only 5 scenarios, but each of them has 3 different plots/objectives and multiple starting combinations.
Components are also really good: the artwork on tiles recreates the perfect lovecraftian atmosphere and the minis are awesome, very detailed.

With soft lights and a right (horror) soundtrack, Mansions of Madness represents an experience.

It enters brutally into my personal top 10.

RATING: 6.6
I don't know what to think about this game: if you play with the right people, you can feel the background (if you play as keeper, you can create an incredible atmosphere/ if you play as investigator, you can have fun in finding the clues of the story).
If you play with the wrong people, the experience will be very different (not in a positive way!). If the keeper plays too much aggressively the investigators are very likely to lose.

My vote is  8.5 with the right people and 5.5 with the wrong people.

RATING: 6
Game was somewhat a letdown. I really wanted to like it and probably will give it another chance or two. Still, the game has many balance issues and poorly made scenarios. Unless the Keeper chooses to "play it cool" the Investigators don't have a chance. Also the HUGE set-up time is a problem considering the length of the game (and I'm used to long set-up times, I play Arkham Horror).

The all-against-one-aspect isn't really a plus for my group and the lack of exploration is a negative. (The map is pre-built)

Not sure what to think about the puzzles. They slightly distract from the gameplay but I think the people in my group still kinda like them. However they are maybe too easy.

There are lots of good things in the game though. I like the fact that a single monster means trouble. No monster is a pushover, not even the cultists, unless you are packed with guns or a lot of muscle. In unarmed combat a cultist is almost as good as an average investigator. This I like.
Also the variety of monsters. And I mean the variety WITHIN the monster types. The fact that when you see a zombie or a cultist you have an idea of the monsters stats, but since they vary from monster to monster you dont know exactly how much does it take to kill one.
And monsters are scary. Investigators may go mad when seeing them. Especially if the monster is chasing the Investigator. The monsters are thematic gold.

And the combat. And the barriers and hiding. This game has a lot of gold in it but overall the game doesn't really work for our group. Still, I would love to see some of these mechanics in future games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Unique experience. In some ways, a traditional dungeon crawler, on others - a mini rpg experience. Asymmetrical game, where one player is the evil Keeper, and others take roles of Explorers, trying to solve the mystery. While both parties have their own goals, the Keeper usually knows more about the game and thus sometimes take the role of traditional game master. It's up to him, whether it's good to bend the rules sometimes, for the sake of good story and tension. 

Beautifully produced, so many components, figurines, cards, tiles, a feast for your eyes. There are even actual logical puzzles to solve by Explorers. 

The backstories of characters are fine and scenarios are also interesting (with some, though limited, replayability), but it's not a top-notch writing. Still decent, though. 

There is a good chunk of rules to absorb before you start the game. It's best if the Keeper knows the rules and maintains the game flow. Most rules are clear, but there might be some rough edges you need to clarify with faq or some additional questions. Keeper, who sets up the scene before the game, needs to devote some time beforehand, to actually make this right - certain mistakes can ruin the game completely. 

The common consensus is that some scenarios are "broken", that is, played weird or that it's impossible/very hard to win by certain sides. I don't really see it this way. For me, each scenario is an experience to relive. Sure, you want to win, but usually, it doesn't matter that much. All the drama and events unfolding during the game feel far more rewarding that the simple "I win" condition. So if you look for just game mechanics in this, you might have problems with certain parts. 

It reminds me of Claustrophobia, which plays quite similarly and the mechanics are alike, but Claustro is a very streamlined version of Manions of Madness, without all the "epic theme feeling" crust.

I feel that there's a great potential in next expansions and PoD scenarios. Superb game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
The number one game on my must-play (and have) list. Finally I got around to play. I say finally, yet it has only been released for a couple of weeks, but it felt like ages.

I think it is a great experience but like many other themed games, especially the similar Arkham Horror and Betrayal, I found that it depends heavily on who your are playing the game with when it comes to the question of having fun or not.

Luckily, my group was a blast and we managed to steal the victory from poor Mark who had a hard time once we discovered the sinister plan brooding in the mansion filled with maniac hairdressers.

Still, a few things that did not convince me at first play:

A. The puzzles are neat and definitely a nice detour from the common flow of Ameritrash games but I think they generate too much downtime and feel disconnected from the other part of the game.

B. The customization options are too limited

C. In general, the number of available cards in one scenario (as in equipment, events, monsters) is too limited

D. I doubt that this game is highly replayable. I think the designer managed to make it as replayabe as possible considering the storytelling nature of the game but I cannot see this holding up after more than 10 plays (without expansions), which is not my money's worth

E. The scenario felt a little bit scripted and the choices were limited, especially once the victory conditions had been discovered

Apart from that, awesome stuff

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mansions of Madness is a good choice. It takes a few plays to get used to the system, and it's easy to be lazy and not read the cards ahead of time. But once you play a scenario a few times, it's easy to have your RPG gamemastering experience kick in, and see how well you can out-psyche the players and terrify them. Mansions has a *very* nice death spiral, in which the players are slowly whittled down, yet have the clock timer to deal with.

***

Interesting comment about scripting -- many roleplaying game scenarios are written in a scripted ("railroading") manner. Heck. Even the dungeons in "Dungeons and Dragons" are scripted: You can either go left through this door to do something you don't know about, or right through that door to do something that you don't know about. Yeah, those are meaningful choices.

OTOH, scripted adventures are the "baby steps" game masters need to learn before moving on to in-depth adventures. Meanwhile, horror roleplaying games have a twenty-year head start on horror boardgames, so many horror adventures, particularly Chaosiums's Call of Cthulhu adventures, are open-ended, but still have a flowchart style so the gamemaster isn't overwhelmed with possibilities.

So, hopefully, just because *these* MoM scenarios are scripted, doesn't mean we won't have *any* MoM scenarios that aren't. Unlike most boardgames, MoM (as well as Descent, most RPGs, and even CCG-style card games like Dominion) is a *system* and it's important to distinguish this component of the game from the "implementation" (ie. scenarios, quests, and actual cards that come in the set).

Anyway, here's a link to Chaosium's "Call of Cthulhu" roleplaying game freebie. IMO, *No* boardgame even approaches the depth and immersiveness of the CoC RPG, which also has some of the best horror adventures out there. You should be able to easily use the props (tiles and miniatures) of MoM in the CoC RPG game as well. Heck, that's a third of the reason why I'm getting this MoM.

But skip over the introductory scenario, The Haunting. It's too scripted. :goo:

Chaosium's Call of Cthulhu quickstart:
http://www.chaosium.com/article.php?story_id=87

***

I'm banging together a roleplaying game system that combines Mansions of Madness with Arkham Horror. Here's the overview:

1. Get a Call of Cthulhu RPG adventure. Chaosium.com has a free Call of Cthluhu RPG download if you're not familiar with the system.

2. Adapt the outside parts of the adventure to the AH gameboard locations. Investigators move up to two spaces then have an action, typically an encounter or an additional space of movement. If they are in a location which has no activity mentioned in the adventure, draw an AH encounter card. Use MoM's skill roll system and the MoM monsters.

3. Inside locations use the MoM game system. Either adapt an existing scenario, or make your own. 

For those who aren't familiar with Call of Cthulhu RPG adventures, most of the investigation takes place before entering the abandoned house. This includes the location of the house, history of the house, and hopefully better knowledge of house to defeat the evil inside of it. Even if you don't plan to play the RPG, the adventures are *great* reading.

***

The maniac "regenerates", so you can stock up on threat early in the game. Have the maniac attack the combat guy *before* they find the shotgun. Have monsters pop in and out of rooms to force horror checks. Reduce investigators down two one health then kill them off once the Objective is revealed so they're not replaced. Hammer one investigator until he or she is a burden to the party. Play Kleptomaniac on the non-combat character so he/she steals the weapons. Have the maniac grab samples from the investigators and get to the altar for more threat. Otherwise, you use monsters to delay the investigators until time runs out. 

Best way to win the game is to out-psyche the players. If you *really* want to win, read the cards thoroughly and study the map so you know how to use the decks.

****

I usually have one of the players help out with setup. Just hand them the cards and tell them which room to put them in. Speeds up setup, although canny players will know the "random card" rooms vs. the preselected ones. It's not rules overload for the players. The Keeper *must* have a good handle on the rules, but I've introduced this game to casual gamers.

The game *is* long by BGG standards, but not Ameritrash. I prefer it to Call of Cthulhu RPG since I don't have to be "nice" to the players. The rules do a good job on letting me be as eeeeevillll as I want without this boring "investigators survive" nonsense!

*****

And if you want the unpainted miniatures, there's the Mansions of Madness game itself. Metal monster miniatures aren't cheap -- two Shoggoths and two Cthonians would cost at *least* $40. That's half the MSRP of MoM, which can be bought from a OLGS for $55.

Rafm Cthulhu prices:
http://www.rafm.com/Merchant2/merchant.mvc?Screen=CTGY&Store_Code=RAF&Category_Code=COCM&view_perpage=all

RATING: 5
A mix of Descent and Arkham Horror, with a touch of Betrayal at House on the Hill. I played as Keeper the first time and really laid it onto the players as much as I could, weary of how you have to fight with tooth and claw in Descent to even dent the players. They were a bit unfocused the first few turns, and so I managed to delay them enough to win.

Comments afterwards were that replayability is questionable. Sure, you can vary the scenarios three ways, but it feels as if it won't be variation enough. One player felt she couldn't do enough with only two moves and one action every turn. Myself, I didn't think much of the roleplay, fluff aspect of the game. Sure, atmosphere paragraphs were okay to read out loud, but I was expecting more than just short descriptions and the main weight laying on game mechanical aspects - like how to optimize who goes where end does what before time runs out. Also, due to time restraints when we set the game up, I didn't read through every card on the board and was as clueless as the players what was about to happen. Rules were a bit tough to grasp the first play-through, but proved very simple and straightforward as we went.

The third game I played, one year later, seemed more fun than I remembered it to be, but quickly turned into a less interesting race - which dragged out to a long and not very stimulating session. It seems as if the Keeper has to decide whether to "roleplay" against and entertain the other players (not compete so much) or play it as a race - a mode in which MoM  frankly gets quite boring.

All-in-all, this is an okay title, but I prefer playing other games.

RATING: 7.5
Thematically, this is a beautiful game. I am a big Lovecraft fan, so that scores it some major points in my books. The map setups, the storyline, the mini-figures (which I painted), and the clues and item cards feel like they were torn from the pages of a Lovecraft story.

That said, while also thematically accurate, the Keeper (bad guy) has an insane advantage over the Investigators in almost every scenario. At the end of a few hours of play having 1 person win and 4 people lose is not fun. Especially when those 4 people had very little they could do to fight back.

My advice, if you do feel the urge to give this game a shot, is to use the new ruleset and cards created by BGG users and posted on the forums here. They do improve balance a great deal, but the Keeper is still likely to win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This is a weird one. It's a game I enjoy playing occasionally - a friend that owns it lets me play as the keeper (or pushes me to play the keeper, depending how much I feel like playing this) - but I'd never buy it. 

So let's start:

Why will I never own it?
Because I think it's a bad product:
1. Replayability is very low. Base game has 5 scenarios with very low replayability (don't play the same one twice in a row), Alchemy expansion has 3 more and Call of the Wild 5 more. Now CotW has scenarios which are not so linear and are more about keeper bluffing the players, so there's some replayability there. Then again it's not one of those games you'll play very frequently. Actually the game doesn't want you to play it very frequently. 
2. The set up time is ridiculous. 60 minutes. No joking. If you organize the components very well you might get it to 30 minutes. One of the offenders here are miniatures which won't go in the box if you put them on stands. As a keeper I'll also need some time to understand what this particular scenario wants of me. Because the time investment of game + set up + preparation (thank god for BGG player aids - FFG why don't you make them?!) this is a game I need some convincing to play. I tend to like it while playing, but takes a while to get there. 
3. The game offers very little return for monetary investment. There's a huge amount of unique items that will only be used in certain scenarios, or only in certain options within these scenarios. And I'm okay with a shitload of cards, but there's a huge amount of plastic minis that don't ever see play. I've played 8 scenarios so far and once I actually managed to get eldrich horror into game (because it was Call of the Wild and they figured out we should get some of this monsters actually in the game). 
4. With all this variabilty the games get kinda samey. Investigators must go from point A to point B to point C to point D. And me the keeper will put bad stuff in the way to delay them. That's the strategy. Base game and Alchemy do have too linear scenarios. But CotW fixed it, at least for those 4 scenarios out of 5 (one is with no keeper, now that's just ridiculous...). 
5. You need Call of the Wild. Seriously, you do. It's that much better. Which just means you need even more money to get started. 

Why I do like to play it and I do enjoy it: 
Atmosphere.
That's it. It's hard to say narrative, but atmosphere and theme immersion are the only thing that the game has going for it, but here it deliveres big time. Essentially it's expensive idiot proof RPG. Preparation time is still shorter than in proper RPGs and there's no creativity required. So when you need something like this (for people who never RPGed, for when you can't find somebody with enough time on their hands to be a GM) it will deliver.   

What I would rather own instead: Betrayal at the House on the Hill. Simpler, much more replayable, and more mindlessly fun. 

Still I do enjoy my time as the Keeper. Mwahahaha! (my success rate is 75%)

EDIT: It's a game with low replayabilty and highly procedural with a long set up time. Great if you like eyecand and so-so if you don't (I don't). It does what it promises to do, but it's just too maintenance heavy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
1 play, good guys

A semi-cooperative one-vs-many game in the Cthulhu mythos, with AWESOME monster miniatures!

The Keeper plays against everyone else. Each side has their own objective to win the game, although only the Keeper knows what those are initially! The Investigators (MANY available from which to choose, each with variable character attributes, and starting items and/or spells) must explore the mansion, find clues, and achieve their objective before the Keeper does. Those clues are either simply informational cards, or puzzles. The puzzles are actual, physical puzzles that must be solved alone by the person encountering them. They all involve moving tiles, changing position or orientation, to achieve the desired solution.

Pretty fun, but some annoying things here, too:
- a lot of downtime for the Investigators, especially if someone is trying to solve a puzzle -- no one else is allowed to say ANYTHING!
- combat involves a whole lot of searching through a deck of cards, one by one ... boring!
- LONG setup
