=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very cool, this game is essentially Descent in the Arkham Horror universe.

One player takes control of the monsters and the dungeon and the players attempt to solve a mystery and otherwise fulfill the requirements to pass a mission.

There's a lot of theme to bite into here.  Each of the scenarios is full fleshed with different clues leading you to others, items and monsters that tie into each of these stories, and options provided to the "Keeper" that branch the story out in different directions and give him lots of fun stuff to do.

The most novel and one of the most fun mechanics in this game are the puzzles that you can solve to overcome obstacles and unlock certain locks in the game.  These are essentially different flavors of sliding puzzles but it's a neat little mini-game that really makes it feel like one of those old Sierra mystery adventure games.

I worry that the content is a little lacking, there are only 4 or 5 different scenarios in the base game and while there are some small variations and branching paths the Keeper can take, playing the same scenario multiple times would lose some of its luster.  This is, of course, a system that you can build your own scenarios into.  I'm not creative but others are and you can find homebrew adventures right here on the Geek.  But I'm sure everything doesn't fit in as nicely scripted in a home-made campaign with the same content.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A lot of fun
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Interesting game.  It uses an "overlord" similar to Descent to run the baddies and the plot.  The other player characters play the role of adventurers taken from FFG's Arkham Horror game.   Players explore a modular mansion to find clues on how to stop....something.  As they explore, stuff happens and the adventurers get more and more freaked out as measured by sanity tokens.  They will most likely also be taking wounds as maniac or zombies or some other horror gnaws/claws/chops/bites the heroes.  The heroes can move, and then get an action which can be run, explore, use and item or attack.  The "overlord" can then act, moving attacking and other stuff.  I don't know what the other stuff is as I only played a hero while someone else ran the overlord.

Unlike both Arkham Horror and Descent, this one can be played in 2 hours.  It has ample replayability by building in a random set-up element in addition to the modular board.  The combat is largely diceless although there is ample D10 based skill checks.  These skill checks can range from attacking to entering a burning room.    The game played quickly, was immersive and had an interesting story.  I can see this game gathering a following similar to Arkham Horror.

RATING: 10
This game is freakin' awesome. It absolutely requires a fair-minded RPG GM type Keeper...but following within the established rules and common sense, you truly get to attempt to achieve TPK (total party kill). The keeper must prepare beforehand, just like a real GM before a role play session, but the rewards are immense.  There are some scenario objectives which shouldn't really be played with being rewritten, but MOST of the base game is pretty well balanced.  The writing could be better and deeper for even more of a Call of Cthulhu role play feel, but even just stock the stories are good...read everything with appropriate feeling of course...but the Keeper is an experienced GM, so it shouldn't be a problem right?

Seriously, if you want the role playing experience, with a fun game on both sides of the GM's screen,  AND you want a full game in less than 3 hours, you simply cannot do better than this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First impression: "Those minis look WAY bigger in person!!"  In fact, "minis" hardly seems like the correct word for them.  Maybe "maxis"?  (No, ewwwww...)  Anyhow, what started out as a bit of an overall "meh" feeling in the first turn of the game ended with a tense, adrenaline-filled finish as we sought to unravel the mystery of the scenario.  By the end of that first play, I was hugely impressed with how the game threw the investigators into this house with almost no knowledge of what was going on, with how the clues helped them to find some direction, with how the investigators had to talk amongst themselves to attempt to decipher the mystery and to work out their various options, and with the overall atmosphere of mystery, suspense, deduction, and cooperation that the game produced.  If I could say anything negative about the experience, it is that the movement around the house (and the subsequent unraveling of the mystery) felt much too linear.  Even though the map gave the initial impression of a large house with a lot of rooms to explore, the way in which the rooms were connected left a very limited pathway for the characters to follow. I would have preferred a much more "open" space for the investigators to explore rather than a "find a locked door, then a key to that locked door, then another locked door, then another key for that door..." and so on.  Perhaps other scenarios are more open-ended, but I felt somewhat too "channeled" in this one.  As for the components, in typical FFG fashion, the minis are spectacular.  The board looks great, although I wish the doors and blocked areas were more clear at a glance.  In other typical FFG fashions, the card text was frustratingly miniscule, and there was too much of a reliance on dozens of tiny cardboard bits rather than on charts or the more newly fashionable dials that could have made upkeep less tedious.  It certainly appears that just setting up a scenario would be a mammoth undertaking and that the keeper player has a veritable plethora of details to keep track of, although once the game is under way, play is relatively simple for the investigator players.  In a nutshell, this game feels like a much more structured Betrayal at House on the Hill to me, which is a very positive thing.  This is a game that I would have very likely picked up 3 years ago, but what prevents me from purchasing it now are 2 young children and a mortgage that keep both my budget and my playing time very limited, and which unfortunately mean that it could be months before I find the time commitment required for a play of this very fun game.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 4/5  Total: 8/10

RATING: 3
This game has beautiful artwork and story line.  That is all.  

The game is horribly unbalanced. Several of the rules are flawed and many issues are unaddressed.  Any number of monsters fit in a room despite size.  Cultists create sanity checks for horror when they are merely just bad people. Monsters can walk freely through investigators but investigators suffer attacks of opportunity if they want to move through or even leave a space with a monster without attacking.  Plus more...

The tokens don't make much sense, the sanity damage tokens are singles but the physical damage have values, which I guess helps keep track of monster health but is cumbersome when trying to find them in all the mess.

The pieces are excellently sculpted. But while made of a durable and flexible resin, the softness of the materiel allows them to bend making painting them impossible since the paint will flake off, and if left in heat, like the car, they will flex and sag and stay deformed.  Also they never seem to stay on their bases and due to their flexibility makes gluing them to their bases nearly impossible as well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
How in the world do you rate this game? Its got cool minis you aren't supposed to paint. Its got fast play but agonizing setup time. Monsters have neat variety for what they do in combat, but are bafflingly the same outside of a fight. Characters have lots of options, many of which don't matter. It drowns you in flavor text while practically fighting against actually producing immersion or narrative. Played with a keeper playing as a "GM" it can be incredible fun. Played competitively, like its supposed to be, it looks like the game could easily collapse with a variety of degenerate strategies in some cases, while games would be pathetically hopeless for the keeper in others.

Story 4 made me lower my rating. Losing sanity due to boredom is not a good simulation of the Cthulhu mythos.

Story 5 dropped my rating to a 1. Broken in multiple ways. I mean completely broken - the scenario we played, the Keeper cannot possibly accomplish the objective (unless the investigators randomly decide to do it for her).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This game suffers greatly from many little rules that does nothing for the game except slow it down.
The monsters are many and have all different attacks and rules, and players have different things they can do, and different things can happen, and there are several conditions (like darkness) and items that counter that condition. But all of those things are just minor variations of the same thing (special attack of zombie 1 does 1 more damage than special attack of identical zombie 2, but that requires a different test than zombie 3. All these things just add up to a bunch of little rules that slow the game down without adding anything to the game.

The game is also seriously flawed with the whole role playing game and competative game hybrid. The game master tries to do his best to win, but he knows the victory condition for him and the players, but the players does not know their victory condition. The game master knows everything that is going to happen, but the players does not know anything. So it seems like a good game master can plan for everything, but the players just have to end up doing random stuff that seems good without any knowledge. Also one of the characters has a broken ability that is: Roll a die, if you roll low you win the game (you get to know your objective of the game which you should not know until you have explored the whole house several turn later) And this you can do at turn one, which actually means that the game can be over before it even begins. (Turn one use ability ->success->Do objective which might be do an aciton in the starting position->Win at round 1)

But if you just want to mess around, roll some dice and do a little role play on a board without caring about winning at all it can be a fun game. (But then I would rather play the role playing game)

RATING: 5
I love Ameritrash game, as long as the "Ameri" half has the upper hand on the second word...

This game starts from a wonderful idea: let's mix Arkham Horror with Descent. The problem is that, just like Descent, it seems to have been not playtested enough.

It's full of logical absurdities (monsters i.e. brainless zombies that pass through doors locked and closing doors behind them), of needless dice rolls, often totally pointless or with minimal effects on the game, and so forth.

A big occasion totally wasted imho. Of course, great flavour + wonderful miniatures + good hype = instant hit.

As for myself, I'll stick to my old Arkham Horror.

RATING: 9
The thing about FFG games is that they do not only give you extremely detailed and wonderfully crafted plastic arguments to buy them, but also: there's a lot of game to them. The puzzle-mechanic is a stroke of genius. It gives you a mini-game in the game. Kind of reminds me of the feeling when in a recent videogame you get to play some vintage video games. The whole game is dripping with theme and flavor. It's always thrilling. Admittedly, our hosts had the best ever soundtrack for this game possible (Bass communion - Ghosts on magnetic tape). And that boosted the experience even further. The investigators won the game by one health point. Reportedly, in an earlier session the keeper won by exact the same margin. I already adressed the awesome miniatures, but of course, the whole production quality is amazing. You wouldn't expect anything less from FFG. With games like this one and Sid Meier's Civ (and all the other great stuff), it's hard to not be a fanboy.

RATING: 3.5
Weight: Rulebook tries to be very light but due to compromises and omissions is extremely heavy.

Rules: Extremely poorly written, although marginally better than Arkham Horror. Rules are scattered very illogically around rulebook and distributed in various cards. Rules and especially cards contain many outright errors that require tedious reading and corrections or other fixes. 

Components: Relatively nice (except rubbish figures) but many of the cards are unnecessary clutter, and there could have been much more efficient methods available.

Update: There are way too many cards on the table and sorting takes really, really long while. 

Scenarios have obvious flaws and outright errors (excluding illogicalities). Most scenarios are seriously broken and in many the story does not carry. Game is playable at times and with liberal application of interpretations and their variance from game to game. 

Update: Many house rules have been suggested to fix the most critical bits (to make game and scenarios at least playable), but it would be easier to rewrite the whole instead implementing them all on top of current ruleset. 

FFG has elected not to care.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
My group were veterans of Arkham Horror, which we played for the first 1.5 years of our gaming lives almost exclusively before branching off into other board games. As I was the dedicated fan of Lovecraftian things in the group, I decided to pick up Mansions, a decidedly-slightly-less laborious version of Arkham Horror. I also love campy horror, particularly the kind similar to the first Biohazard game. A game based only on exploring a campy horror mansion? Count me in!

In execution, the game recycles much of AH's mechanics in a room based setting rather than a large city. While by no means a bad game, some of the GM's cards are particularly overpowered, and my group quickly figured out that the only way the GM can really lose on at least half of the scenarios is he if makes particularly bad choices or lets the players win. While the stories are the centerpiece rather than the gameplay, it requires a dedicated game master. The figures that make up the majority of the components are also a major pain to store, made much worse by the addition of the expansions.

RATING: 9
Need: FFG 6 Yellow 3 Green
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
There are some elements of this game I do enjoy and appreciate. Cthulhu-themed games are a major plus and this game really goes all out in creating a Lovecraftian horror story. Thee battle/ spell casting mechanics where  different outcomes are possible is a nice touch.The production quality of Mansions of Madness is top-notch. The art by Henning is gorgeous and the cardboard components are really thick. The unpainted miniatures are well made  the fact they allow the creature stats to be slotted beneath the miniature is a nice touch.

The reason I cannot score this game any higher is mostly due to my own experience .The gameplay in Mansions of Madness itself favours the Keeper/ dungeon master. Scenarios are created with investigators at a significant disadvantage. Investigators are frequently racing against the clock and against the keeper who has all sorts of ways to delay you. From summoning monsters constantly, forcing you to slowly but surely lose sanity to locking all important doors and forcing you to go through a specific series of events before anything happens.
If you would ever want to pick up this game, make sure that the people you play with are those that value the story-telling element of the game and not winning all the time. And avoid a keeper that reminds you that he 'Helped' a lot by not playing more severe trauma cards or summoning more monsters than what he did so you would "have fun".

My experience with this game is limited to playing the base game but that is enough to turn me off from the Mansions of Madness franchise. When playing with others, make sure that the role of the keeper is that of a story-teller rather than one that is more intent on winning, as this WILL ruin the experience for the other players.In my games the keeper killed my character in EVERY game (and I was blamed for selecting a weak character) and mocking bad dice rolls (and in this game, bad rolls will be frequent when the keeper plays trauma cards on you). In a game where the characters are already facing difficult challenges, a Keeper of this level of competitiveness can ruin the experience for the investigators and leaves a bad aftertaste.

If your group is not highly competitive and enjoy thematic games with VERY high production quality, then by all means get it. Otherwise steer clear.

RATING: N/A
Horrific monsters and spectral presences lurk in manors, crypts, schools, monasteries, and derelict buildings near Arkham, Massachusetts. Some spin dark conspiracies while others wait for hapless victims to devour or drive insane. It’s up to a handful of brave investigators to explore these cursed places and uncover the truth about the living nightmares within.
\13\\13\
Designed by Corey Konieczka, Mansions of Madness is a macabre game of horror, insanity, and mystery for two to five players. Each game takes place within a pre-designed story that provides players with a unique map and several combinations of plot threads. These threads affect the monsters that investigators may encounter, the clues they need to find, and which climactic story ending they will ultimately experience. One player takes on the role of the keeper, controlling the monsters and other malicious powers within the story. The other players take on the role of investigators, searching for answers while struggling to survive with their minds intact.
\13\\13\
Do you dare enter the Mansions of Madness?

RATING: 7
I [i]really[/i] didn't intend to compare this to Betrayal at House on the Hill, but there's no getting around it.  Both involve haunted house settings where crazy stuff happens and a story unfolds.

The unflattering aspect of the comparison is that, stacked up against Betrayal, Mansions is twice as long and ten times more painful to set up.  In its favor, though, MoM is enough of its own beast that the existence of one shouldn't preclude the existence of the other.

An apt comparison between the two games is that Betrayal is like a Saturday morning cartoon: short, silly, light, and almost completely random.  MoM, by comparison, is an involved, engaging, and purposeful full-length feature.

But the strong thematic similarity is still potentially off-putting, which is unfortunate. Honestly, I think that it's a case of bad timing.  While MoM was under development by Fantasy Flight, they couldn't have figured that Betrayal would come back into print so soon (mistake #1).  Other more problematic issues are the numerous errata sheets (one shipped in the box, another one available for download; mistake #2) and an overly-scripted storyline (mistake #3). It doesn't take a genius to parse the heavy-handed hints and deduce where the next clue should be.

Nitpicks notwithstanding, there is a great deal of innovation that MoM brings to the table: nifty spatial puzzles, a coherent story from the get-go, weapon-specific (and often spectacularly awesome) descriptions of combat effects, plot thickenings, a sense of real danger when the party splits up to explore, and one-off flavor events that provide excellent detail and context (e.g., having a character break a leg). Together, these add up to great escapist entertainment.

Whether the game's fun is sustainable from scenario to scenario is something that I'd like to explore - and hopefully the bubble won't burst in only a month or two.

RATING: 10
"There was an old man named Kent, 
Who came from the village of Brent. 
He rejoiced at the night, 
For the stars where right. 
Chanting Ph'nglui mglw'nafh Cthulhu R'lyeh wgah'nagl fhtagn as he went."

[i]UPDATE AUG 2012:[/i]
My rating of "9" was preliminary.

Further exploration of the game, especially including the [i]Forbidden Alchemy[/i] expansion, has revealed this game to be a breathtakingly ambitious design - that I for one, love wholeheartedly. Even though I dread setting the bastard up, the game deserves a "10" rating. Hardcore gamers should regard this as a totemic item, worthy of worship.

Twenty years ago, [i]Mansions[/i] would have been regarded as a world-beating masterpiece. I fear nowadays, this design might have been overlooked in some circles - those people are wrong. And not just because I say so.

If you love the Cthulhu dressing, crave a diet-RPG adventure - and aren't intimidated by some fiddly rules and systems, you will adore what is on offer here. Play this on an unhurried evening (maybe once a month) with three other like-minded friends, a full set of painted miniatures, and Ryleh may just rise for you.

[b]In relating the circumstances which have led to my confinement within this refuge for the demented, I am aware that my present position will create a natural doubt of the authenticity of my narrative.[/b]

1. Amazing theme. I have loved Lovecraft's scribblings since I discovered his non-euclidean works at age twelve or so - offbeat, overwrought horror stories packed with mysterious ancient tomes written by mad Arabs, hysterical folk driven insane by terrible revelations and strange beasts, and a pantheon of tentacled gods the likes of which man was not meant to know. Nowadays, more wonderful than the lore of old men and the lore of books is the secret lore of boardgames.
2. A very deep, heavy box of goodness is exchanged for your coin. And it's adorned with a superb painting, I might add.
3. Fantastic miniatures - the sculpts are very detailed and in great, iconic poses. You also get quite a few of the bleeders for your delectation, each with a system of clever figure-base slots. Slide into it a monster chit describing on the underside a powerful figure-specific power, on the front the beast's two ratings (horror and awareness); all this is easy to see for the players without book-keeping or stat-cards. Nice.
*I am almost salivating in anticipation of painting the cultists (in a Resident Evil 4 style, of course) and eldritch monsters.
4. Smooth and elegant rules. With some very minor referencing of the rulebook during play proper, I'd say a competent gamer can run a game without too much loss of insanity.
5. Smart design. Real smart. Professor Pabodie of the Lake Expedition smart. Unique and radical in its capacity to foster an atmosphere of tense, exciting, thrilling exploration and pulp adventure. This deserves to be a huge success and make everyone involved a coffin full of used bucks. Might be one of the most influential games ever designed, only time will tell. I don't like everything that Corey has accomplished, but this is his masterpiece.
6. In the main, the game is simple and fast to run. You may have to read the rules regarding evade and horror checks a few times, but not much else besides. Turns speed past quickly, with little or nothing outstaying its welcome.
7. Hugely fun for the keeper player. I happen to like setting rooms ablaze and turning out the lights while manoeuvring a mewling Shoggoth toward a half-insane investigator (in the game-world!). You know, it was a lot of fun just closing in on the players with mad, dagger-wielding cultists, even before summoning any horrors from beyond. After thinking about it for a bit, I also believe that you have to play the keeper [i]slightly[/i] like a storyteller. Even though you're a team unto yourself, out to win the game, I think if you break into smithereens everything the players find and use, afflict them with every malady at your disposal, break their legs, poke them in the eyes, throw paintings at them with poltergeist activity, you [i]may[/i] find that they don't want to play again. All the fights with monsters are fair game, I would add, just don't beat the investigators black and blue throughout the whole game with interrupt cards. Who doesn't hate it when their character is moved by the enemy? Oh, that would be everyone.
8. Tactical and strategic decisions are available for both teams. You feel like what you do makes a difference, and that every move and action counts, especially towards the end.
9. Seems to scale well for 2-5 players. The jury is still out on the 2-player experience, but the investigator player could play more than one character easily, if that is what you chose to do.
10. Mini-games. Oh the mini-games! These add enormously to the unfolding narrative, providing fun relief, and a pinch of jeopardy to the proceedings: try fixing a fuse and wiring puzzle with a abomination from another dimension shambling toward you!
TIP 1: put a teacloth over unfinished puzzles - so that players don't figure out the puzzle when they're not supposed to.
TIP 2: I beseech you to use the optional 60-second time limit for puzzle solving. It worked in Space Hulk, and boy does it work well here.
11. Well written rulebooks. Mostly. It took me two readings of the rules for me to grasp every pseudopod of the design. However, I would have liked some detailed notes on the scenario set-up pages, regarding the mission-specific rules. For instance, the ladder in [i]The Inner Sanctum[/i] comes in two parts, but the second half is not revealed until later in the game. If I had not puzzled this out before play, I may have panicked slightly during a rushed set-up, flipping backwards and forwards furiously through the booklet looking for a side-note that does not exist. We only played one rule wrong during our first game: the flipping of combat cards and which titles trigger a result. By this I mean, we played that a "sharp-edged weapon" only read out the "sharp-edged weapon" combat card results. You do this, but you must also use the generic "melee weapon" combat card results. I reminder in the rules might have solved this minor issue.
12. Feels like a semi-RPG experience - and that is not something I say lightly, but you do hear it all the bloody time these days and mostly it is not the perfect truth. More so than most any other boardgame I have played, this crafts a fascinating narrative that grips the players and forces them to concentrate on a story. Great stories they are too. Some internet parrots will squawk that you should just crack out the Cthulhu RPG if you demand that kind of game evening. Well, yes, you [i]could[/i] do that. But sometimes I just want to watch a movie, and not wade through an entire TV series, even if it's a brilliant one.
The atmosphere is augmented massively by the copious amounts of flavour text on everything. It's great to read out a small plot before the main game begins, and then a prologue specific to the keeper's story decisions. All this is for naught, and the entirety of the game, if your group does not read out aloud the flavour text on each and every card. Do not buy this game (it is not for you) if you intend to play it by picking up a card and saying "move back 2 spaces", instead of reading the card: "You back away in horror as a pair of blood-red eyes approach you from the gloom..."
13. Brilliant art on the cards. Even if they are recycled from other FFG Cthulhu projects, I don't care.
14. Each room tile is a work of art, drawing you into the Mansions of Madness with resplendent chapels, cobweb strewn attics and tiled halls. They are very well rendered, I must say.
15. Expandable. You will want more of every damn thing. You could expand the combat decks with new descriptions of severing and whatnot, you could add extra charcters to increase choice. New room tiles depicting a clifftop, or a summoning circle in a nearby wood, or a conservatory attached to the mansion, or a sewer system, or a [insert almost any good 1920's horror location], would be welcome. New scenarios using the rooms you already have would be smashing. New monsters, obviously.
16. It's a cooperative team game! All the investigator players try to solve the clues to the unholy mystery together. They win or lose as a team.
17. Tension. Due to the event deck system, and the keeper placing clock tokens on top of the deck, you have a built-in timer mechanism - this obviously fosters tension. As the clock ticks, nasty, bad things happen. Better get a move on, chaps! The ground is shaking, and I hear funny things.
18. Game machinery. The gears and workings of this remind me of Horus Heresy (just a taste) and Fury of Dracula, mixed with a dungeon-crawling game.
19. Pace. We know that the pace of the game is largely dictated by the keeper's actions, but the restrictions on what he/she can do and when (threat tokens and scenario-specific keeper action cards), mean that the game feels a helluva lot less like a crap dungeon game (Descent) and more like a RPG. To be clear, I mean that you're not fighting every turn, at least not at the start of the adventure. There is time to explore, and not be harassed every turn by an axe-licking nutcase - though that may happen! Also, the monsters in Mansions have a bit of weight to them, unlike most of the horde in Descent. It's good to have to regard each monster as a real threat, not as an approaching balloon ready for the investigators to pop.
20. Arkham Horror. One of my biggest gaming disappointments was Arkham Horror. Overwrought, overcomplicated, messy, with amazing fluff gaffer-taped all over it, but that's just my opinion. I'm a Cthulhu junkie, owning all the novels and virtually every Cthulhu RPG book produced, so I was coming at AH with the eyes of a dedicated fan, and it still fell way short of my expectations and desires. Mansions of Madness is superior to that game in every way that matters to me.

[b]The yawning lightless gulf of N'kai - beneath Mount Voormithadreth, the four-coned volcano.[/b]

1. The figures are unpainted. So you will have to learn fast or pay somebody that does know how to bring these miniatures to life. The immersion in the game will be hugely enhanced by a full set of painted models, I guarantee you.
2. Each game will probably take 150+ minutes.
3. The owner of the game is highly likely to become the keeper of the scenarios, more times than not, due to the very nature and structure of the game.
4. Large amount of set-up and putting away time is required. This is due to the large quantity of parts (cards and tokenry) that keep the game-engine running.
5. Prodigious number of baggies required. Opening and closing these puppies (of Tindalos) will keep somebody busy before and after play. A good solution is a pill box or some-such.
6. A fair bit of dice rolling is necessary. This adds tension in places, and you can increase your odds of success by spending skill points - of which you don't have many. Just letting you know, because some gamers don't like dice (Philistines!).
7. I can see that some players may be put off by the inclusion of mini games. They really shouldn't be afraid of this design choice. They are a radical idea, granted. For me, the mini-games are a crowning achievement. Yes they require different thinking than is usually applied during other boardgames, but I do not feel that they distance you from the rest of the game's undertakings. Some fear that puzzling over a puzzle, alone, without any help from the others might be embarrassing or humiliating. I don't play with boneheads so I don't foresee this as an issue. Actually, scratch that, not everyone is good at abstract quizzing who enjoys a good theme-rich game. I still think they will like this aspect when they have actually tried it.
8. Pricey. Worth every hard-earned coin.
*Not sure why folk whine about the price - in the manner that they feel they should get more in the box. Hey, I realise not everyone has a suitcase full of cash to spend on boardgames in these hardened financial times, but I know people who spend double the cost of Mansions on one night of hard drinkin'. Put it this way, I think you should realise that you're getting the design for the price, not the box of plastic monsters. I don't buy a Blu-ray and say: "In this box I only got the disk". Behind that disc, in the film's production, could have been years of hard toil and craft (say Avatar, Beowulf, or Tron: Legacy). Surely it's the content that ultimately matters here, isn't it? I'd pay the same price as Mansions for just a card game - if it was the world's best card game, mind you. What I am explaining badly is that the "game" is more important to me than the pawns and cubes in the box, but I might be a rare breed.
9. The box insert has failed its SAN roll.
10. It's a cooperative team game! This fact alone will pour on a certain amount of hate.
11. Replayability. I don't see replayability as an issue. Also, if someone else wanted to be the keeper of a scenario straight after I'd just ran it as the keeper, because he can tweak the plot, I'm certain I'd still have a load of fun as an investigator. This has been a key criticism of Mansions since the first news, and it is mostly unjustified, How often do you really play your games? I've looked, and some of you have 300+ games - you can't tell me you've play all of them the 20+ times or so it would take to get bored of them. Same here. I reckon I will be able to play what I have in the original box many, many times on either team before getting weary. And that's not counting on me playing the same set-ups with a different game group. You could play the same scenario with different folks and have a totally separate experience.
12. Cannot be played solo. That would drive you insane, if you dared to attempt it.
13. Pre-game preparation. I strongly advise any potential keepers to prepare a scenario pack, with all your decisions made and all the cards and tokens needed for the quest inside. This way, all you need do is build the adventure from your pre-made pack. I would not attempt to play the game at a convention or game-meet without preparing something like this beforehand.
14. Stress. Playing as the keeper could be stressful (the ever-present fear of cocking up the game by incompetent secret play).
15. Revealed rooms. Sure, you're missing a little of the theatrics you would get if the mansion chambers were revealed one-at-a-time, but what you get instead is a crackerjack story-driven venture. The game would progress at a baby crawl if you had to set up each map-tile as you went along, shotgun or torch in nervous hand.


In summary, does the game achieve its objectives, as stated by Corey in his scribbled notes? A resounding yes! Mansions of Madness is fantastic, and the Cthulhu game I have been awaiting for many years.



I'll leave you with a conversation overheard between a cultist and one of his robed buddies:

"Cthulhu seems like kind of a wuss if he can be trapped by a sinking island or killed by a boat." 

"That’s just because the stars aren’t right. When the stars are right, it don’t matter how many boats hit him. He’ll sink whole continents and lick off the people like salt off a pretzel." 

"Says you." 

"You keep talking smack like that, he’s gonna eat you first."

RATING: 6
I wrote the very first review of Mansions of Madness on BGG:
http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/621934/impressions-after-playing-first-game

However, now that I have had the opportunity to play this more, much of my enthusiasm has waned.  This is a game where the sum is less than the parts.  Lots of neat mechanics - combat cards, choosing options for each scenario, puzzles and locks, etc.  Yet when I put them all together, the game is just fair.  Part of the problem is that the options for each story end up making the final story seem like a series of random events.  Each scenario starts off with an interesting premise, but then you just get stuck doing the same old puzzles and locks, bouncing from one part of the board to another to get keys to open up locks, etc.  While the combat decks look interesting at first, once you look at those that actually apply - say a person with a blunt melee weapon, he just keeps dealing with the same handful of cards so the novelty wears off.  I feel I should love the game, but keep finding it somewhat disappointing.  It would really help if the individual rooms had more flavor text like they did in the old descent or even Arkham encounter cards.

RATING: 10
Great game with loads of flavor text and very story driven. It's modeled after H.P. Lovecraft's books set in the fictional city of Arkham in Massachusetts. Two to four investigators pick a story to explore and with clues form the keeper, they're attempting to unlock the mystery and escape/win before time runs out.

This game is very similar to Descent: Journeys in the Dark and another major reason I enjoy it so much.
